Stocks Manage to Outrun Poor Data
Thursday's Markets
Stocks Manage to Outrun Poor Data
By Brendan Conway
464 words
13 January 2012
The Wall Street Journal
J
C4
English
Copyright 2012 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Stocks eked out a gain as investors shrugged off three tepid readings on the state of the U.S. economy to
focus on the coming rush of corporate earnings.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average rose 21.57 points, or 0.2%, to 12471.02, closing at the highest level since
late July for the second time this week. The Standard & Poor's 500-stock index gained 3.02, or 0.2%, to
1295.50, its fourth advance in a row. The Nasdaq Composite rose 13.94 points, or 0.5%, to 2724.70, the sixth
consecutive gain, the technology-oriented measure's longest win streak since early July.
The more cyclical materials and industrial sectors pulled the market to the upside, offsetting weakness in
energy. Stocks recovered from early-session losses that followed disappointing readings on weekly jobless
claims, December U.S. retail sales and November business inventories. Strong bond auctions in Italy and
Spain helped to boost sentiment.
"We're not seeing this market sell off on bad news," said Jonathan Corpina, senior managing partner at New
York Stock Exchange floor broker Meridian Equity Partners.
Financial stocks, which have been among the market's strongest performers this year, pared losses to finish
with a gain ahead of J.P. Morgan Chase's earnings report Friday. J.P. Morgan finished up 19 cents, or 0.5%,
at $36.85.
Energy stocks were weak after Chevron warned investors it expects fourth-quarter 2011 earnings to be
significantly lower than in the preceding quarter. Chevron fell 2.80, or 2.6%, to 104.97, the biggest decliner in
the blue-chip Dow.
In addition, oil tumbled $1.77 to $99.10 a barrel, thanks to reports the European Union's Iranian-oil embargo
could be delayed.
European markets erased early gains and finished mostly lower. The Stoxx Europe 600 shed 0.2%, despite
the two well-received bond auctions.
Gold futures settled 0.5% higher at $1,647.30 a troy ounce, the third gain in a row. The dollar lost ground
against the euro and the yen. The 10-year Treasury yield rose to 1.935%.
Early Trading in Asia
Asian stock markets rose Friday on news of successful debt auctions in Europe and as the European Central
Bank left interest rates unchanged.
Japan's Nikkei Stock Average rose 1.3%, Australia's S&P/ASX 200 added 0.4%, South Korea's Kospi
Composite was 0.7% higher and New Zealand's NZX-50 gained 0.2%.
Cyclical stocks rose on improved risk sentiment, with BHP Billiton up 1.3% and Rio Tinto 1.1% higher in
Sydney. Toyota Motor added 2% and Sharp gained 1.6% in Tokyo. In Seoul, Posco climbed 1.4%.

Global Fears Rattle The Dow
Wednesday's Markets
Global Fears Rattle The Dow
By Jonathan Cheng
392 words
12 January 2012
The Wall Street Journal
J
C4
English
Copyright 2012 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Stocks finished the day roughly flat, as concerns over a weakening economy in Europe gave investors pause
after the market's recent gains.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average declined 13.02 points, or 0.1%, to 12449.45, snapping a two-day winning
streak.
The Standard & Poor's 500-stock index inched up marginally, or 0.40 point, to 1292.48, while the Nasdaq
Composite added 8.26 points, or 0.3%, to 2710.76, to extend those benchmarks' string of gains. The Nasdaq
has risen for five consecutive sessions.
Leading the Dow's decliners were energy and consumer-staple stocks. Coca-Cola dropped $1.28, or 1.9%, to
$68.06; Chevron lost 1.29, or 1.2%, to 107.77; and Exxon Mobil fell 64 cents, or 0.8%, to 85.08.
Materials and financial stocks limited some of the losses. Bank of America climbed 24 cents, or 3.6%, to 6.87;
J.P. Morgan Chase rose 61 cents, or 1.7%, to 36.66, and Alcoa added 19 cents, or 2%, to 9.63.
The cautious move came as investors reassessed the recent run-up in stocks. In the past 15 trading
sessions, the Dow has risen nearly 700 points, or 6%.
The pullback came amid fresh signs Europe's economy is slowing, adding to fears about how the debt crisis
there plays out.
European stock markets finished with modest declines. The Stoxx Europe 600 fell 0.4%, and France's
CAC-40 index shed 0.2%.
Gold futures rose 0.5% to $1,639.20 an ounce, and crude-oil futures shed 1.3% to settle at $100.87 a barrel.
The euro fell to $1.2706, from $1.2778 Tuesday.
The yield on the 10-year Treasury note fell to 1.905%, its lowest level this year.
Early Trading in Asia
Asian stock markets were mixed Thursday, as fresh signs of stress in Europe's economy and caution ahead
of inflation data from China kept most buyers at bay.
Japan's Nikkei Stock Average was down 0.8%, Australia's S&P/ASX 200 was flat, South Korea's Kospi
Composite added 0.1% and New Zealand's NZX-50 lost 0.6%.
Exporters were under pressure. In Tokyo, Toyota Motor fell 0.5% and Canon lost 1.4%.

Investing in Funds: A Quarterly Analysis --- Mixing It Up: Advisers Brace for Another Volatile Year --- How do you prepare? Pros at four...
Investing in Funds: A Quarterly Analysis --- Mixing It Up: Advisers Brace for Another Volatile Year ---
How do you prepare? Pros at four firms talk about how they're tweaking their portfolios to get ready
By Shefali Anand
1,568 words
9 January 2012
The Wall Street Journal
J
R11
English
Copyright 2012 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
The U.S. economy will likely grow modestly this year, but global uncertainties and the U.S. presidential
election could keep stocks volatile, say some financial advisers across the country.
As a result, some advisers are cautious when it comes to the stock market, especially after a period of sharp
losses last year, when the Standard & Poor's 500-stock index fell 18% between late July and early October.
While it recovered afterward to end the year flat, the downturn made individuals, especially retirees, very
nervous.
"This high level of volatility is starting to change the comfort level of individuals," says Brad Cougill, chief
investment officer at Deerfield Financial Advisors Inc. in Indianapolis.
In this column, we feature model portfolios from prominent financial advisers who invest in mutual funds and
exchange-traded funds. We reconnected with advisers from Deerfield Financial and three other firms we
featured in the past two years to see how they navigated 2011 and what they expect this year.
Here are their views:
Brad Cougill and Dick Bellmer
Deerfield Financial Advisors Inc.
If U.S. stocks provide mid-single-digit returns in 2012, it would make Dick Bellmer, founder and president of
Deerfield Financial, a happy man.
Mr. Bellmer and his colleague Mr. Cougill are worried about the longevity of the U.S. economic recovery.
They note that although consumer spending in the U.S. has gone up, real income hasn't, which means
people are likely digging into their savings to spend. That may not be sustainable, suggests Mr. Cougill. He
adds that while corporate profits are high, it's mainly because companies have cut expenses. "Can you
continue to get growth in the absence of a growing economy?" he asks.
In addition, the advisers think the presidential election could be a damper on stocks. "Markets don't like
uncertainty," says Mr. Bellmer.
Given this outlook, the Deerfield advisers have cut back on their clients' stock allocations and have added to
bonds. "Not that we think that fixed income is going to be great over the long term," says Mr. Cougill. But they
want to get safety for the next two years. "If we're wrong, we'll give up some of the upside" if stocks gain, he
says.
For a client who can handle moderate risk, the advisers now allocate 15% to U.S. stocks, down from 25% last
spring when Deerfield was featured in this column. Foreign stocks now get 8.5% versus 13% previously.
Bonds get 68% compared with 55%, and the rest goes to a real-estate and a commodities fund.
In early 2011, Deerfield advisers were buying selected high-quality municipal bonds, because their prices had
been beaten down over fears of potential defaults by municipalities and other issuers. "The defaults have not
picked up," says Mr. Bellmer.
The average intermediate-term municipal-bond fund gained 9% last year, according to Morningstar Inc. "The
overall value in the municipal world is still there," says Mr. Cougill.
Nelson Lam
Lam Group Inc.

--- Page 8 ---
Page 8 of 202 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
To get an indication of which investments might do well in 2012, Nelson Lam, an investment adviser in Lake
Oswego, Ore., is looking at what experts say is the stupidest thing to do this year. He says that is often a
good contrarian indicator.
As an example, he notes that at the beginning of 2011, experts talked about the risk of rising interest rates
and said the stupidest thing to do would be to buy long-term government bonds. But last year, long-term
government-bond funds and those which invest in Treasury inflation-protected securities were among the
best-performing funds, returning 33% and 11% on average, respectively, according to Morningstar.
Similarly, in early 2009, just months after the collapse of investment bank Lehman Brothers, many experts
said it was time to cut back on stocks and other risky assets, says Mr. Lam. But in the two years after March
2009, "risky assets outperformed all others," says Mr. Lam.
More recently, Mr. Lam says investors are talking about European stocks as being the worst place to be,
given the myriad problems facing those countries. He says he's seriously considering adding to clients'
holdings in broad international funds that own Europe, to bring them back to target allocations.
The unpredictability of returns reinforces Mr. Lam's belief that individuals are best off diversifying among
various asset classes.
For clients who can handle moderate to aggressive risk, Mr. Lam allocates 21% to U.S. stocks; 29% to
foreign stocks, including small foreign companies and those in emerging markets; 36% to bonds, including
foreign bonds; and 14% to real estate and commodities-linked funds.
Mr. Lam didn't make any changes in the allocation last year but did buy or sell investments when they
deviated widely from their targets. "The need to rebalance has become greater because there have been big
moves," Mr. Lam says.
For 2012, he says, "I am optimistic but uncertain -- which is why [we need] global diversification."
Tim Medley
Medley & Brown LLC
"I think the next two years will be very good," says Tim Medley, founder of financial advisory firm Medley &
Brown LLC in Jackson, Miss.
Mr. Medley believes that the U.S. has around two years of economic expansion ahead. He points to the
strong balance sheets of U.S. companies and their low stock prices as reasons for his optimism.
"In the next couple of years, we will see housing construction come back," he adds. When that happens, he
believes, it will add jobs and contribute further to the economy and ultimately to the stock market's
performance.
Mr. Medley says history is on the side of equities, as they have provided a 10% annualized return over the
long term. "If we only get halfway there, it will be 5% -- still a handsome number compared with today's
choices," such as low bond yields, he says.
Mr. Medley has only modestly tweaked his client portfolios since late 2010. He currently invests 58% in
U.S.-stock funds, 30% in foreign-stock funds and 12% in bonds for clients five or six years away from
retirement.
Unlike many other advisers, Mr. Medley doesn't invest in funds dedicated to commodities or real estate or
other alternative investments. He says he leaves it to his stock-fund managers to decide if there's any need to
invest in these sectors. Some of them own oil- and real-estate-related stocks.
In late 2010, Mr. Medley had advised against loading up on gold, which had run up more than 20% in the
prior year. In 2011, gold gained 10% to trade at $1,565.80 an ounce, but Mr. Medley stands by his earlier
decision. He says that gold doesn't have inherent returns and has been driven up by fear in recent years
rather than by investment fundamentals.
He says he would rather buy high-quality U.S. stocks for a five- to 10-year period.
Kathy Muldoon
Carter Financial Management
This year could be good for "value," or cheap, stocks, says Kathy Muldoon, partner at Carter Financial
Management in Dallas.

--- Page 9 ---
Page 9 of 202 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Ms. Muldoon notes that many large companies like Microsoft Corp., Procter & Gamble Co. and Target Corp.
are trading at low prices when compared with their earnings. "I'm hopeful that those values are recognized in
2012," she says. To benefit from this, she has added a tilt toward value in her client portfolios.
For a moderate-risk client, Ms. Muldoon allocates 28% to U.S. stocks (including 15% of the total portfolio in
value stocks), 17% in foreign stocks and 35% to bonds and cash. The remaining 20% goes to alternative
investments, such as commodities funds and those which aim to make money in up and down markets.
Ms. Muldoon has maintained this broad asset allocation since early last year but has tweaked several fund
holdings.
Like many advisers in early 2011, she was concerned about a potential rise in interest rates, which would
hurt bond prices, so she had allocated 9% of the portfolio to floating-rate-debt funds. However, after the
Federal Reserve said in mid-2011 that it would hold rates flat for two years, Ms. Muldoon sold these and
bought Mainstay Municipal High Yield, a fund that buys municipal junk bonds, and Sentinel Total Return,
which currently holds a lot of cash and bonds that mature in less than five years.
Ms. Muldoon bought EIC Value, a stock fund launched last spring by Equity Investment Corp., whose private
funds Ms. Muldoon has owned before. She likes that the fund's investment strategy includes an aim to avoid
significant losses.
Last year, Ms. Muldoon stopped committing new money to Oakmark International because its investments in
European stocks caused the fund to lag behind its peers substantially over three to four quarters in 2011. She
believes the European recovery may be a long while coming. For clients who already owned the fund, she's
holding the investment.
She says these changes are meant "to lower the risk profile of the portfolios overall."
---
Ms. Anand is a personal-finance columnist for The Wall Street Journal, based in New Delhi. Email:
shefali.anand@wsj.com.

Australia's Economic Luck May Not Last
Australia's Economic Luck May Not Last
By Philip Bowring
711 words
6 January 2012
The Wall Street Journal
J
A11
English
Copyright 2012 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Euro-zone chaos, a U.S. debt downgrade, Japanese disaster, Chinese doubts and Indian disappointment.
Few countries ended 2011 in the good books of commentators, but one does stand out: Australia boasts
above-average growth, a strong currency and a stable banking system. And with a relatively low
trade-to-GDP ratio, it is supposedly less vulnerable to global shocks.
But 2012 may be the year when the gilt comes off the lucky country. Look at what's happened to its major
export prices since 2002. Iron ore is $138 a ton today, up from $13 a decade ago, and thermal coal is now
$115 a ton compared with $29. Gold is selling for $1,600 an ounce against $280. Liquefied natural gas (LNG)
is currently up only 50% at $120 per thousand cubic feet but has been above $450.
The gains since 2002 are only sustainable if the business cycle has been repealed.
Coal and iron ore are in abundance around the globe but transporting them required new ports and railways,
which are now being built. Copper and gold are less abundant, but with prices now more than double
marginal production costs, dozens of new or enlarged projects are in train -- many funded by China. LNG
prices have already collapsed, and if fracking goes ahead at a fraction of its potential, producers could be
waiting a decade or two for a new price boom.
The producers' assumption is that additional demand from China will continue to keep prices buoyant. But
China's growth is slowing and anyway will move gradually from materials-intensive infrastructure to
consumption and services. India and other developing countries should see steady growth, but that will
probably no more than offset stagnation in developed-world demand for materials.
All this should be worrying for Australia, where the consensus believes that any softening in mineral prices
will be offset by increases in Australian supply. But commodity prices always move much more violently than
changes in demand. How would Australia's trade look if gold were back at $800, coal and iron ore at $60?
The gain that Australia has enjoyed in its terms of trade, which has underwritten so much else, would quickly
vanish.
Australians should be doubly concerned because an export price boom bigger than in the 1970s has still
seen the country averaging a current-account deficit equivalent to 3% of GDP. Even 2011, with its record
mineral prices, will probably record a 2% deficit.
It is often assumed that current-account deficits are normal as Australia is a young nation that can put foreign
capital to good use. But Australia is not a developing economy anymore. It is an aging developed country. Its
accumulated foreign liabilities now total $848 billion, or 60% of GDP, of which $740 billion is net debt. Its bank
loans are 40% funded by borrowings of around $300 billion from overseas -- not a comfortable position when
global interbank markets are so nervous.
What's more, a high proportion of bank loans are to households with the highest debt-to-income ratios in the
developed world, thanks mostly to very high housing prices. A very strong Australian dollar -- almost double
its level against the U.S. dollar of a decade ago -- has hurt manufacturing and tourism.
For sure there are cushions. The government has little foreign debt. The banks' foreign currency borrowings
are hedged. The freely floating currency acts as a shock absorber. There is scope to reduce interest rates.
Household savings have risen for the past two years and the big mining companies are cash-rich and can
mostly finance expansion from their own resources. Australia remains a politically safe refuge for Chinese
and other new money.
But the bottom line is that Australia is more vulnerable than is usually assumed. Certainly more so --
excluding governance issues -- even than Indonesia, which has a healthier trade balance, much less foreign
debt, better demographics and a broader economic base. As for China, a commodity-price retreat could more

--- Page 11 ---
Page 11 of 202 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
than offset stagnation in Western markets and provide a noninflationary stimulus to domestic demand. What
is now good for China is no longer good for Australia.
---
Mr. Bowring is a Hong Kong-based journalist.

COMMODITIES
Indian Cabinet Is Seeking
A New 'Gold Standard'
NEW DELHI -- Jewelry buyers across India may soon be able to get a guarantee of the quality of the gold
they are paying for, with the federal cabinet approving an amendment to a law that could lead to compulsory
hallmarking.
Government officials say traditional handcrafted pieces, with their many joints and alloys used for soldering,
leave room for dubious claims on caratage. Currently, hallmarking of gold jewelry is optional for retailers.
The amendment has to be ratified by Parliament before it becomes law. "This will take at least seven to eight
months," a top government official said Wednesday.
-- Rajesh Roy and Biman Mukherji
---
EUROPEAN DEBT
U.K. and Germany Find
Buyers in Bond Auctions
Germany kicked off the euro zone's bond sales of this year with a successful sale of 10-year government
paper, just six weeks after a poor auction fanned fears that the bloc's debt crisis was hitting its safe haven.
The government sold 4.057 billion euros ($5.28 billion) of the 2% January 2022 bund, its current 10-year
benchmark, with bids reaching 5.14 billion euros. The German Finance Agency had offered 5 billion euros of
debt.
The average yield was 1.93%, in line with levels in the secondary market and down from 1.98% in late
November. After the auction, the yield fell to 1.918% in late European trade.
Separately, the U.K. received strong demand at its first gilt auction of 2012. The U.K. Debt Management
Office said the GBP 3.75 billion ($5.87 billion) reopening of the 1.75% January 2017 gilt received a
bid-to-cover ratio -- a gauge of demand -- of 2.02 times. The yield tail, or the difference between the highest
accepted yield and the average yield, came in at 0.004 percentage point. The gilts were sold at an average
price of GBP 103.17 and an average yield of 1.102%.
-- Emese Bartha and Tommy Stubbington

IN THE COURTS
Alleged Social-Media Scam
Leads to Charges by SEC
The Securities and Exchange Commission charged an Illinois-based investment adviser Wednesday with
offering to sell more than $500 billion in fictitious securities through various social-media websites such as
LinkedIn.

--- Page 13 ---
Page 13 of 202 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
The SEC attached an alert on social-media use by advisers to its news release about the charges against the
investment adviser, Anthony Fields.
It also attached a similar alert for investors with tips to help avoid fraud online.
In its case against Mr. Fields, the SEC alleges the Lyons, Ill.-based adviser used LinkedIn discussions to
promote fictitious "bank guarantees" and "medium-term notes," resulting in interest from multiple purported
potential buyers. Mr. Fields couldn't immediately be reached for comment.
-- Caitlin Nish

GREEK WOES
Credit Agricole Gives Cash
To Emporiki Bank Unit
PARIS -- French bank Credit Agricole SA said it had pumped a further 2 billion euros ($2.61 billion) into its
ailing Emporiki Bank of Greece unit to support it against the country's continuing economic woes.
Credit Agricole said the capital increase won't hurt itself financially as it involves converting an advance made
previously to the Greek bank.
"This capital increase has no impact on the solvency ratio of the Credit Agricole group. This is an internal
operation, which consists in converting shareholders' advances and which started back in 2010," a Credit
Agricole spokeswoman said Wednesday.
-- Noemie Bisserbe and Stelios Bouras

Temasek has been drawing on executives from banks and other companies as it seeks to create a deeper
leadership base at the state investment firm, which had a portfolio of US$150 billion as of March 31, 2011, up

Blue Chips Hold On for Slim Gain --- Dow Industrials Edge Up 21.04 Points in Wake of New Year Rally; 'Microcosm of What We Saw in 2011'
Wednesday's Markets
Blue Chips Hold On for Slim Gain --- Dow Industrials Edge Up 21.04 Points in Wake of New Year Rally;
'Microcosm of What We Saw in 2011'
By Tomi Kilgore and Steven Russolillo
681 words
5 January 2012
The Wall Street Journal
J
C4
English
Copyright 2012 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Blue-chip stocks managed to eke out a gain after rallying in the previous session, as encouraging results from
auto makers helped offset euro-zone liquidity concerns.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average rose 21.04 points, or 0.2%, to 12418.42. Wednesday's gain came after the
Dow surged 179.82 points on Tuesday, the third-biggest point gain to kick off a new year in the index's
history.
The Standard & Poor's 500-stock index edged up 0.24 point, or 0.02%, to 1277.30, its third gain in the last
four trading days. Consumer discretionary and material stocks rose, while health care and utilities fell. The
technology-oriented Nasdaq Composite slipped 0.36 point, or 0.01%, to 2648.36.
"The first two days of 2012 have been a microcosm of what we saw in 2011: risk on and risk off," said John
Carey, portfolio manager at Pioneer Investments. "We're in that funny period where we don't have much in
the way of concrete corporate news. People are clutching at straws in the absence of corporate earnings."
Alcoa rose 22 cents, or 2.4%, to $9.45 and Microsoft gained 64 cents, or 2.4%, to 27.40, to pace the Dow's
gains.
Verizon Communications shed 52 cents, or 1.3%, to 39.21. The company reported it sold 4.2 million iPhones
in the fourth quarter, a quarterly record for the wireless carrier.
Encouraging results from some auto makers helped buoy sentiment. Ford Motor reported a 10%
year-over-year increase in vehicle sales for December while General Motors reported a 4.6% gain. For all of
2011, Ford sales increased 11% and GM's rose 13%. Both auto makers see sales gains intensifying in 2012.
Ford rose 17 cents, or 1.5%, to 11.30. GM advanced 10 cents, or 0.5%, to 21.15.
Meanwhile, European markets pulled back from the recent string of gains amid concerns over liquidity among
euro-zone banks. Overnight deposits at the European Central Bank reached an all-time high, suggesting
banks remain more willing to park money at the ECB than to lend to other banks.
In addition, Spanish bond yields rose. A report in Spain's Expansion newspaper said the government is
considering applying for loans from the European Union's rescue fund and the International Monetary Fund
as it moves to restructure Spain's banking sector.
The Stoxx Europe 600 slipped 0.6% after rising 4.5% over the previous four sessions. Asian bourses were
mixed, with Japan's Nikkei Stock Average gaining 1.2% on its first trading session of 2012, while China's
Shanghai Composite shed 1.4%.
The dollar advanced against the euro and fell slightly versus the yen. Crude oil rose 26 cents, to $103.22 a
barrel, and is up nine of the last 11 sessions. Gold advanced for a third consecutive day, up $12.20, to
$1,611.90 a troy ounce.
In corporate news, Yahoo named Scott Thompson, the president of eBay's PayPal division, as its new chief
executive. The appointment comes after the Internet company fired its previous CEO, Carol Bartz, in
September. Interim CEO Tim Morse will resume his role as chief financial officer. Shares shed 50 cents, or
3.1%, to 15.78.
Netflix said its members watched more than two billion hours of TV shows and movies through its streaming
service during the fourth quarter. Shares rose 8.21, or 11%, to 80.45.

--- Page 15 ---
Page 15 of 202 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Early Trading in Asia
Most Asian stock markets dropped in early trading Thursday as euro-zone concerns resurfaced, tempering
sentiment following decent gains at the start of the year.
Japan's Nikkei Stock Average fell 0.6%, Australia's S&P/ASX 200 dropped 1.2%, South Korea's Kospi
Composite rose 0.4% and New Zealand's NZX-50 lost 0.4%.

Year-End Review of Markets & Finance 2011 --- Protests, Crises and Celebrated Exits
Year-End Review of Markets & Finance 2011 --- Protests, Crises and Celebrated Exits
By Brian Hershberg and Michael Driscoll
5,801 words
3 January 2012
The Wall Street Journal
J
R20
English
Copyright 2012 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
The annals of history will show that 2011 was a year in which societies met some of their worst nightmares
head on. Resolution, however, may take years.
In Japan, that meant dealing with the devastating trio of an earthquake, a tsunami and a nuclear meltdown. In
parts of the Arab world, that meant rising up against longtime tyrants and demanding freedom. In Europe, that
meant contending with a metastasizing debt crisis that threatens to tip the region into recession and undo a
currency. And those are just a few of the more high-profile events.
At home, a shooting in January killed six and wounded U.S. Rep. Gabrielle Giffords and others. In May, U.S.
forces raided a compound in Pakistan and killed al Qaeda head Osama bin Laden after a long hunt. Summer
was filled with partisan bickering over the typically procedural matter of raising the debt ceiling, with an
11th-hour vote averting default but costing the U.S. one of its triple-A ratings. And the autumn boiled over with
the loosely affiliated protest movement Occupy Wall Street expressing popular discontent across the U.S.
Through it all, the world said goodbye and good riddance to leaders reviled by many -- Egypt's Hosni
Mubarak, Tunisia's Zine el-Abidine ben Ali, bin Laden, Libya's Moammar Gadhafi and North Korea's Kim
Jong Il. Others were close to leaving as the year ended.
Here is a summary of news events from The Wall Street Journal. In most cases, the dates reflect when the
items were published, not the date of the event.
JANUARY
4 Mark Zuckerberg's Facebook raised $500 million from Goldman Sachs and others, in a high-profile private
offering. Goldman later had to amend the offering, which raised $1.5 billion all told and valued the company at
$50 billion, to avoid running afoul of U.S. securities rules concerning private ownership.
Republican Rep. John Boehner took over as House speaker.
8 On this day, Rep. Gabrielle Giffords, an Arizona Democrat, was shot in the head as she spoke with
constituents outside a supermarket near Tucson. Six others were killed in the attack. Jared Loughner was
later charged with murder and dozens of other counts.
12 China has launched trading in the yuan in the U.S. for the first time, an explicit endorsement of the
fast-growing market in the currency.
15 Pope Benedict attributed a miracle to John Paul II, advancing the case to have his predecessor declared a
saint in record time.
18 Tunisian politicians formed a caretaker government. The new cabinet, which will run affairs until new
elections are held, followed the overthrow of longtime President Zine el-Abidine Ben Ali -- the first leader in
the broader Mideast felled by a popular uprising in nearly two decades.
Steve Jobs is taking an unexpected leave from running Apple.
20 The GOP-led House voted 245-189 to repeal the health-care law. The Senate didn't take up the measure.
21 Federal agents conducted a record mob sweep. Morning raids in New York, New Jersey and Rhode Island
netted 119 people. In all, 127 people were charged with crimes including fraud, loan-sharking and murder.
Google co-founder Larry Page was tapped to take the reins as chief executive from Eric Schmidt in a surprise
change atop the Internet giant.

--- Page 17 ---
Page 17 of 202 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
26 Thousands of protesters clashed with police in Egypt. The demonstrators called for the ouster of President
Hosni Mubarak, an end to Egypt's emergency law and an increase in the minimum wage. In the days, weeks
and months to follow, popular unrest would spread across the region.
28 Hedge-fund manager John Paulson personally netted over $5 billion in profits in 2010, likely the largest
one-year haul in investing history.
31 Syria's president said he will push for political change. Bashar al-Assad acknowledged that Arab rulers
would need to do more to accommodate their people's aspirations amid the unrest in Egypt, Tunisia and
Yemen.
FEBRUARY
1 A U.S. judge ruled the health overhaul is unconstitutional because it requires Americans to buy insurance,
in a blow to the law. Weeks later, another judge upheld the law.
4 A whistleblower group says in a lawsuit that Bank of New York Mellon currency traders created fake trades
and overcharged Virginia pension funds by at least $20 million.
6 On this day, the Green Bay Packers win Super Bowl XLV over the Pittsburgh Steelers, 31-25.
8 J.P. Morgan planned to accept gold as collateral in some transactions, adding to a movement that places
the metal in the top tier of asset classes.
9 On this day, plans emerged for Germany's Deutsche Borse to acquire NYSE Euronext, operator of the New
York Stock Exchange. The deal would later face resistance as European regulators expressed competition
concerns.
11 On this day, Egypt's Mubarak stepped down as leader of the Arab world's most-populous country, handing
power to the military. At least 846 Egyptians were killed during 18 days of protests that ousted him, a
fact-finding committee later said.
14 China passed Japan in 2010 to become the world's second-largest economy, after the U.S.
15 President Obama unveiled a $3.73 trillion budget that would close or reduce 200 federal programs but
make only a modest dent in long-term deficits.
16 A funeral in Bahrain swelled into growing clashes with security forces, in the first Mideast unrest to pit a
Shiite majority against Sunni rulers.
17 Borders filed for bankruptcy protection. The bookstore chain later entered liquidation.
23 Col. Moammar Gadhafi vowed to fight on as a rebellion that had been creeping for days swept across
Libya. The unrest threw Libya's oil industry into turmoil as ports closed and foreign companies suspended
operations.
24 The Obama administration, in a reversal, said it believed a law defining marriage as the union of a man
and a woman violated the Constitution.
25 The death toll reached at least 110 from an earthquake that devastated Christchurch, New Zealand. The
year closed with strong aftershocks.
MARCH
4 President Obama said publicly for the first time that Gadhafi must give up power. Later in the month, the
U.S. helped lead creation of an air shield over Libya that eventually came under NATO auspices.
5 The U.S. jobless rate fell below 9% for the first time in nearly two years.
8 Charlie Sheen was fired from the Warner Bros. sitcom "Two and a Half Men" amid controversy over alleged
drug use and inflammatory statements.
Subway surpassed McDonald's as the world's largest restaurant chain, measured by number of outlets.
10 Illinois Gov. Quinn signed a bill abolishing the death penalty, making his the 16th state to end capital
punishment.
11 On this day, Japan was struck by the most-powerful earthquake to hit the country on record, triggering a
devastating tsunami. Thousands were killed and billions of dollars in damages resulted. A nuclear crisis later

--- Page 18 ---
Page 18 of 202 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
developed at a stricken plant. Three days later, in the first trading session since the disaster, Japanese stocks
slid 4.4%.
15 Warren Buffett's Berkshire Hathaway will pay $9 billion for chemical maker Lubrizol, the investor's latest
foray into the industrial sector. At the end of the month, David Sokol, a leading contender to succeed Buffett,
resigned amid revelations about trading stock in Lubrizol.
17 Data showed U.S. sales of Diet Coke overtook those of Pepsi-Cola for the first time in 2010.
18 G-7 nations banded together to stem a rise in the value of the Japanese yen, the first time they have
joined to intervene in currency markets since 2000.
19 AT&T reached a deal to acquire T-Mobile USA from Deutsche Telekom for $39 billion, a combination that
would create the nation's largest wireless carrier. But the proposed tie-up buckled later in the year amid
competition concerns from the Justice Department and was abandoned.
22 Citigroup will reinstate its dividend at a penny per share after a reverse stock split to cut its shares
outstanding to 2.9 billion from 29 billion.
23 Died: Elizabeth Taylor, 79, the violet-eyed actress who enchanted movie audiences -- and a string of
husbands.
26 Died: Geraldine Ferraro, 75, the first woman to appear on the presidential ballot.
31 A departing federal bailout official told a House panel that firms deemed "too big to fail" remain a threat to
the economy in TARP's wake.
A Russian investor paid $100 million for a Silicon Valley mansion, the highest known price paid for a
single-family home in the U.S.
APRIL
2 Nasdaq OMX and IntercontinentalExchange announced a $11.3 billion offer aimed at sinking a deal for the
New York Stock Exchange's parent company to be bought by Deutsche Boerse. The duo went hostile before
the Justice Department nixed the deal on monopoly grounds.
5 The White House said the alleged 9/11 plotters will face military trials at Guantanamo, scrapping its effort to
try them in civilian courts.
7 Portugal said it would ask the EU for a financial bailout, becoming the third nation in the 17-member euro
zone to turn to its peers for help.
8 Europe's central bank became the first monetary authority in a major developed economy to raise interest
rates since the global financial crisis struck. The ECB increased its benchmark by a quarter point to 1.25%.
12 Japan boosted its nuclear-accident severity rating to a 7, which indicates a major release of radioactive
materials on par with the Chernobyl crisis. Government officials later said decommissioning the Fukushima
Daiichi plant will take up to 40 years.
14 Military factions in Yemen clashed, killing one person. In Syria, protesters called for the release of
detainees.
President Obama called for Congress to cut the nation's deficit by $4 trillion over 12 years by adopting a mix
of revenue increases and spending cuts.
15 The FAA's top air-traffic-control official quit amid a controversy over controllers dozing on the job.
18 China stiffened banks' reserve requirements as Beijing battled inflation.
19 S&P lowered its outlook on U.S. government debt to "negative" from "stable," intensifying divisions over
how best to tackle deficits.
20 Raul Castro was named leader of Cuba's ruling Communist Party.
21 The U.S. will scrap the color-coded terror-alert system and replace it with one designed to give the public
more-specific information about "credible" threats. Later, the White House said in its first formal
counterterrorism-strategy paper that Al Qaeda and its adherents remain the No. 1 security threat to the U.S.
23 Tens of thousands protested in Syrian cities. Security forces in several cities fired live ammunition and
used tear gas to try to disperse the crowds, leaving at least 70 people dead, according to activists.

Page 19 of 202 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
27 Sony said a hacker had obtained personal data for 77 million people who play online videogames through
its PlayStation Network.
28 CIA Director Leon Panetta succeeded Robert Gates as defense secretary, while Afghan war commander
David Petraeus took over as head of the spy agency.
Obama released his long-form birth certificate to prove he is a native-born citizen, in a bid to stop a fringe
issue from moving mainstream.
Hamas and Fatah reached a power-sharing accord, ending a four-year rift between the Palestinian factions.
29 On this day, the Royal Wedding between Prince William and Catherine, Duchess of Cambridge went off
with barely a hitch.
30 Syrian demonstrators defied a military crackdown. More than 50 people were killed, activists said, while an
opposition group called on the military to take control in an attempt to bridge the gap between regime backers
and protesters. The U.S. announced sanctions against senior Syrian officials, including members of the
president's family.
MAY
1 Late on this day, Americans learned Osama bin Laden was killed during a raid by U.S. forces in Pakistan,
where the al Qaeda leader was found living just 40 miles from the capital.
2 Federal officials assessed damage in the South from storms that brought flooding and tornadoes. More
than 350 people were killed. Further deaths followed later in the spring, when storms struck the Midwest.
3 Silver prices tumbled 7.6% and gold dropped 1%.
5 Scientists ended a 52-year experiment that affirmed Einstein's theory of relativity.
6 Elvis left the list of the 1,000 most popular U.S. baby names for the first time since 1954.
10 S&P cut Greece's credit rating.
11 On this day, hedge-fund founder Raj Rajaratnam was convicted on all 14 counts of securities fraud and
conspiracy. He later received a prison sentence of 11 years, the longest ever imposed in an insider-trading
case.
Microsoft agreed to buy Skype for $8.5 billion, heading off a planned IPO by the Internet-communications
firm.
12 Toyota's share of the global auto market slipped to third place as the company, formerly No. 1 in the
industry, was hurt by a stronger yen and the March earthquake that hobbled plants in Japan.
14 On this day, Dominique Strauss-Kahn was arrested in New York on sexual-assault charges. Days later, he
resigned as managing director of the International Monetary Fund. Prosecutors eventually gave up the case,
saying the accuser's damaged credibility would have had a "devastating" effect at trial.
17 Google launched its first debt issue, $3 billion in bonds, as it joined other technology companies that have
been cashing in on low interest rates to raise capital even as they sit on cash piles.
20 Obama pledged support for Arab uprisings and called for the first time to begin talks for a Palestinian state
based on Israel's pre-1967 borders. Benjamin Netanyahu rejected the position, saying it would leave parts of
Israel "indefensible."
LinkedIn's shares more than doubled to $94.25 in their opening day of trading.
24 California was ordered to reduce prison overcrowding. The decision grew out of lawsuits alleging
treatment provided to mentally and physically ill inmates was so poor as to violate the constitutional ban on
cruel and unusual punishment.
25 In a speech to a joint session of the U.S. Congress, Israeli's Netanyahu rejected compromise on most of
the issues at the heart of the Arab-Israeli dispute. He ruled out the division of Jerusalem, the return of
Palestinian refugees or the withdrawal of all forces from the West Bank.
American International Group and the U.S. Treasury sold $8.7 billion in shares in a stock offering that
garnered a small profit for taxpayers and began the government's exit from the insurer 2 1/2 years after its
bailout.

26 UBS planned to separate its investment bank and incorporate it outside of Switzerland in an effort to

and penalizing those that hire illegal immigrants.
31 Germany said it will close its nuclear reactors by 2022. The move will make Germany the first major

JUNE
1 Home prices in the U.S. were shown to have fallen to 2002 levels, effectively wiping out almost a decade's

3 The USDA replaced its food-pyramid guideline with a food plate that emphasizes fruit and vegetables.

13 The Dallas Mavericks won their first NBA title. Dirk Nowitzki was named MVP as the team took the crown
with a 105-95 victory over the Miami Heat.
16 Pandora's shares rose 8.9% in their first session, down from an initial pop of 25%, amid a broader market

U.S. births fell in 2010 for a third-straight year, according to preliminary CDC figures.
The Boston Bruins won the NHL's Stanley Cup, beating the Vancouver Canucks 4-0 in Game 7.

23 Obama ordered the withdrawal of 10,000 troops from Afghanistan this year, with remaining surge forces

Alleged crime boss James "Whitey" Bulger, on the FBI's Ten Most Wanted List since 1999, was caught.
24 The U.S. and 27 other countries agreed to release 60 million barrels of oil from strategic reserves,
temporarily driving down oil prices to a four-month low in a controversial bid to prop up the global economy.
25 A same-sex marriage law was approved by the New York state Senate. The first weddings under the law
took place in July.
JULY
1 Hugo Chavez disclosed he has cancer. The Venezuelan president made the admission in a televised
speech from Cuba, where he had surgery.
6 A scandal at News Corp.'s News of the World in the U.K. swelled with allegations of the possible hacking of
a murdered teen's phone. The tabloid was later closed. And News Corp. founder Rupert Murdoch apologized
to victims of phone hacking during a U.K. parliamentary hearing later in the month, but he deflected blame for

A jury acquitted Casey Anthony of killing her two-year-old daughter in a case that became a cable-TV

Portugal received a blow as Moody's downgraded its sovereign-debt rating to junk status.

18 Obama said he would nominate former Ohio Attorney General Richard Cordray to lead the new consumer

Syrian forces moved on two border towns and arrested a leading writer after the opposition made a first

27 McDonald's planned to fill its Happy Meal boxes with apple slices and smaller portions of French fries, the

AUGUST
1 Rep. Giffords, the Arizona Democrat who was critically wounded in a shooting in January, made her first

4 Japan intervened in currency markets to stem the rise of the yen against the dollar.

enough to address the long-term picture for America's finances.
9 U.K. police struggled to contain rioting and looting across mostly poor and minority urban areas in the

12 Attorneys general in Virginia and Florida filed civil suits against Bank of New York Mellon alleging the bank

18 Nine weather disasters in 2011 have each caused more than $1 billion in damages, the National Weather

22 Gadhafi lost his grip on Tripoli as Libyan rebels flooded the capital, encountering little resistance from the
strongman's defenses.
26 Bank of America said it will get a $5 billion infusion from Warren Buffett, giving the banking giant a jolt of
confidence as investors question its financial health.
27 Hurricane Irene menaced the Eastern seaboard, leaving dozens dead and millions without power. It
largely spared New York after an unprecedented shutdown of the nation's biggest city.
31 On this day, solar-panel maker Solyndra announced plans to file for bankruptcy. The firm had received
hundreds of millions in federal loan guarantees. Executives later pleaded the Fifth rather than testify about
the company's collapse.
SEPTEMBER
3 A U.S. housing regulator filed lawsuits against 17 financial institutions, saying they sold $196 billion of risky
home loans to Fannie Mae and Freddie Mac without adequately disclosing risks.
6 Obama's approval ratings fell to their lowest level to date. A Wall Street Journal/NBC poll found 44% of

OCTOBER
1 One of the U.S.'s most-wanted terrorists, Anwar al-Awlaki, was killed in Yemen in what the U.S. said was a
CIA drone strike.
4 An Italian appeals court acquitted American student Amanda Knox of murdering her roommate in 2007.
Sprint is making a multibillion-dollar gamble that access to Apple's iPhone will be the ticket to a turnaround for

Year-End Review of Markets & Finance 2011 --- World's Woes Leave Lasting Scars --- Economic Deleveraging and Political Paralysis Across U.S...
Year-End Review of Markets & Finance 2011 --- World's Woes Leave Lasting Scars --- Economic
Deleveraging and Political Paralysis Across U.S., Europe in 2011 Give Markets Little Wiggle Room in
2012
By Tom Lauricella
1,545 words
3 January 2012
The Wall Street Journal
J
R1
English
Copyright 2012 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Investors go into 2012 hunkered down, frustrated and skeptical.
This dour demeanor comes after a year where many investors learned they had underestimated just how
volatile and unpredictable life would be as the world's major developed economies contend with a mountain
of debt.
As the new year starts, there are signs of life in the U.S. economy and glimmers of hope that the European
Central Bank is slowly moving to contain a growing credit crunch. Stock investors remain optimistic that the
strength of U.S. corporate earnings will continue to keep that market in mostly positive territory for the fourth
straight year.
But few investors are getting their hopes up. Gone are their expectations that European officials will provide a
quick fix for the debt crisis. They also remain frustrated by a perceived lack of leadership among politicians in
the U.S. and elsewhere when it comes to making difficult decisions about fiscal and economic policies.
The consternation reflects the world order since the financial crisis, as economies and markets remain reliant
on extraordinary measures by central banks to pump cash into the financial system. But such
market-propping measures can't last forever, and investors fret that central banks are low on tools to offset
further economic drag as years of accumulated consumer and government debt are worked down.
"We really have had quite a difficult environment . . . and I'm not sure we can see this improving dramatically
in the near term," says Richard Bell, co-chief investment officer at London-based bond manager Rogge
Global Partners.
If there is a common theme among analysts' forecasts for stocks, commodities and currencies, it is to brace
for more of the kind of wild swings that were the hallmark of 2011.
"We have to work our way through a lot of volatility, a lot of uncertainty," says Derek Young, head of the
global asset-allocation division at Fidelity Investments.
While the Dow Jones Industrial Average ended the year with a respectable 5.5% gain, more representative of
broader-market performance was the essentially unchanged reading on the Standard & Poor's 500-stock
index. That flatline result masked the market's sharp ups and downs, however. The S&P 500 had been up as
much as 8.4% in late April and down nearly 13% for the year at its 2011 nadir in early October.
European stock prices, meanwhile, were clobbered amid the debt crisis, with most indexes posting losses
well into the double digits. Italy, where bond yields soared to euro-era record levels, saw its stock market lose
25%. Many emerging-market stock indexes also suffered sharp declines.
U.S. Treasury yields fell broadly in 2011 as investors sought a safe harbor and downgraded expectations for
economic growth. The U.S. Treasury 10-year yield finished the year at 1.9%, down 1.4 percentage points.
Global uncertainty also fueled a stampede into gold for much of 2011. The price of gold soared to a record
high north of $1,888 per ounce in August before a late-year selloff took it to $1,565.80 for a 10% gain on the
year.
The year that just ended was characterized by head-snapping turns in asset prices driven by the latest
headlines out of Europe or Washington. Fund managers struggled as broad swaths of financial markets
moved in lock step as traders swung between "risk on" and "risk off."

--- Page 29 ---
Page 29 of 202 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
"It's just a tough market for stock pickers," says Marc Harris, director of U.S. equity research at RBC Capital
Markets. "Everything acts together and it's all driven by news flow."
Reflecting the volatile landscape, the S&P closed up or down 2% on 35 days in 2011, up from 22 days in
2010. In contrast, the S&P didn't post single move of more than 2% in 2005 and just two in 2006.
The year started on an upswing for stocks, commodities and other riskier investments such as
emerging-market currencies. The catalyst was optimism that the global economy was finally on the mend
from the 2008 financial crisis.
Then a pair of shocks hit. First, oil prices surged as governments in North Africa and the Middle East faced
swift-moving popular revolts. Then, in March, Japan was hit by a devastating earthquake that triggered a
tsunami and, ultimately, a nuclear disaster.
Investors initially dismissed the impact of higher oil prices and the hit to the Japanese economy. But they
misjudged the thin capacity of deleveraging economies to withstand shocks by taking on more debt. By
midsummer, the U.S. economy saw a "soft patch" and faced sliding back into recession.
Meanwhile, it was also becoming clear that Europe's sovereign-debt problems were nowhere near resolved.
Greece teetered on the brink of default and the contagion spread to Italy and eventually France -- part of the
core of the euro zone. Efforts by European authorities to address the crisis repeatedly fell short of promises
and investor frustration with their efforts deepened. Now, Europe is seen on the edge of a potentially nasty
recession.
As if that weren't enough, the partisan divide in the U.S. Congress took the country to the brink of default on
its debt payments in early August, leaving many investors angry over what was seen as unnecessary turmoil
in the markets. That was followed by the first-ever downgrade of the U.S.'s credit rating, by Standard & Poor's,
to double-A-plus from triple-A.
By late in the year, the major credit-raters said much of Europe was likely to suffer ratings downgrades as
well. And many investors say they are braced for France to lose its triple-A rating in the early days of 2012.
As the disappointments piled up, a sea change in investor attitudes appeared to be taking place. "Investors
have finally got the idea of what it really means to have seriously different type of recovery than what we have
seen in post-World War II history," says David Mann, head of research in the Americas for Standard
Chartered Bank. "It's going to be a hard slog before the West manages to get out its problems."
Of particular concern is the way deleveraging feeds on itself.
That is playing out in Europe where the deteriorating credit quality of sovereign debt is hurting European
banks that hold that debt. To offset those losses, banks are shoring up balance sheets by cutting back on
lending and dumping bonds and other assets in the markets.
At the same time, European governments are sharply cutting spending. Austerity is a drag on economic
growth, which cuts tax revenues. That then threatens an even worse fiscal outlook which, accompanied by
ratings downgrades, keeps investors reluctant to buy bonds even as yields rise to what not long ago would
have been attractive levels.
"The cycle is quite a difficult process to break," says Rogge's Mr. Bell.
The gloom hanging over the developed markets is overshadowing the generally more optimistic views about
emerging markets. While many emerging markets are seen as able to counter some effects of slowing
economic growth by lowering interest rates or with fiscal stimulus, a European credit crunch could still have
a meaningful impact because European banks had been big lenders in the developing world.
"The intersection between emerging markets and Europe . . . is so tight that as European economies become
more constrained it will have knock-on effects," says Fidelity's Mr. Young.
At the same time, investors are uncomfortable with the degree to which the course of the markets in 2012
appears largely in the hands of government officials. In the U.S., for instance, policy makers are expected to
keep interest rates near zero for as long as the eye can see.
"You're trying to second-guess politicians and as an investor, that's a really bad position to be in," says Alec
Letchfield, head of U.K. equities for HSBC Global Asset Management in London.
Magnifying the uncertainty for the coming year will be presidential elections in the U.S. and France.
All this has driven many investors into cash and government bonds from the U.S., Germany, Britain and
Japan. Stock investors have been gravitating toward higher-quality, dividend-paying names. Hedge funds are

--- Page 30 ---
Page 30 of 202 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
trading even more rapidly than usual, leaning toward trades that profit in volatile markets and offsetting long
positions as much as possible with hedges, according to analysts and investors.
"The average investor is being very defensive," says Jason Pride, director of investment strategy at
Philadelphia-based Glenmede. In general, he says, "equities may be cheap but the risks are high."
This defensive posturing does create a question for investors: How much bad news is already factored into
the markets?
"The bad news is that the outlook is going to remain challenging, but the good news is that many markets are
pricing in that scenario," says Rogge's Mr. Bell. Rogge thinks investment-grade and high-yield corporate debt,
for example, are attractively priced for a difficult environment.

--- Page 31 ---
Page 31 of 202 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Bridgewater Takes Grim 2012 View --- Hedge-Fund Firm That Has Weathered Crises Well Sees More Slow Growth and High Unemployment
Abreast of the Market
Bridgewater Takes Grim 2012 View --- Hedge-Fund Firm That Has Weathered Crises Well Sees More
Slow Growth and High Unemployment
By Tom Lauricella
1,060 words
3 January 2012
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2012 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Bridgewater Associates has made big money for investors in recent years by staying bearish on much of the
global economy. As the new year rings in, the hedge-fund firm has no plans to change that gloomy view.
Robert Prince, co-chief investment officer at Bridgewater, and his managers at the world's biggest hedge-fund
firm are preparing for at least a decade of slow growth and high unemployment for the big developed
economies. Mr. Prince describes those economies -- the U.S. and Europe, in particular -- as "zombies" and
says they will remain that way until they work through their mountains of debt.
"What you have is a picture of broken economic systems that are operating on life support," Mr. Prince says.
"We're in a secular deleveraging that will probably take 15 to 20 years to work through, and we're just four
years in."
In Europe, "the debt crisis is [a] long ways from over," he says. The economic and financial morass will mean
interest rates in the U.S. and Europe will essentially be locked at zero for years.
In this bleak environment, Mr. Prince says stocks remain vulnerable to "air pockets" from shocks, such as bad
news out of Europe. But for longer-term investors looking out over the next decade, he says, equities may be
a good buy. There is even money to be made in U.S. Treasurys, despite interest rates near record lows, and
gold is likely to resume its climb as central banks print money to bolster their economies. Mr. Prince says.
The views of Bridgewater are keenly watched by other investors, given the firm's elevated status in the
competitive world of hedge-fund investing. Bridgewater's flagship Pure Alpha Strategy fund is considered one
of the top funds in the world. At the end of November, it was up 25% since the start of the year, according to
people familiar with the situation. The average macro fund had lost 3.7%, according to Hedge Fund
Research.
Currently, the fund is positioned for higher gold prices, stronger Asian emerging-market currencies and lower
yields across high-quality government bond markets, Mr. Prince says.
In 2011, it profited from owning gold, but cut back on that position during the third quarter. It correctly pivoted
from being bearish on U.S. Treasurys early in the year to positioning for a rally. It also benefited from rallies in
core European bond markets and avoided ugly losses sustained by other macro funds that had bet the euro
would fall against the dollar. Instead, it rightly bet the euro would fall against the Japanese yen.
Founded in 1976 by Ray Dalio, Bridgewater manages $125 billion and has 1,400 employees. Mr. Prince, 53
years old, joined in 1986. The firm's clients are institutions such as pension funds and endowments, along
with foreign governments and central banks. Pure Alpha has been up each year since 2000 and has recorded
just three negative calendar years since 1991. In 2008, the fund returned 9.4% after fees, and after a 2% gain
in 2009 -- its smallest of the decade -- Bridgewater posted a 44.8% return in 2010.
From its offices by the Saugatuck River in Westport, Conn., Bridgewater plays much the same field as other
macro funds. But it tends to be more diversified than its competitors, with numerous smaller bets across a
host of currencies, government bonds, stocks and commodities.
In a conference room at Bridgewater's headquarters, where the water from the Saugatuck appeared to almost
lap at the glass walls, Mr. Prince paints a grim picture of the challenges facing the U.S. and European
economies.
Recent better-than-expected news on the U.S. economy is unlikely to be the start of a healthy expansion, he
says. The uptick in economic growth has been fueled by a decline in the savings rate, which, without material

--- Page 32 ---
Page 32 of 202 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
income and employment gains, is unlikely to be sustainable as long-term credit growth also remains weak, he
says.
The problem for the U.S., says Mr. Prince, is that it is on the wrong side of a long-term debt cycle. "We were
in a leveraging-up period for 60 years, from the early 1950s to 2008," he says. This debt bubble was
self-reinforcing on the way up, and "when it tipped over, it set about a self-reinforcing process on the way
down."
As evidence for the long slog facing the U.S economy, he notes that the level of leverage, as measured by
comparing household income to net worth, is still higher than it was before 2008.
"The most likely environment is moderate growth with wiggles up and down, and this is one of those wiggles
up," he says.
Against this backdrop, the Federal Reserve will need to do more quantitative easing -- buying of government
bonds -- but Mr. Prince says the purchases will probably be sporadic.
Europe, meanwhile, is headed into a potentially deep recession, with policy makers boxed in by an
interconnected banking and sovereign-debt crisis. "You've got insolvent banks supporting insolvent
sovereigns and insolvent sovereigns supporting insolvent banks," he says.
In the U.S., leveraged investors who can borrow money at rates near zero could find a good deal in
Treasurys, Mr. Prince says.
Mr. Prince points to the example of Japanese government bonds. An investor who was leveraged
three-to-one and bought Japan's bonds at a 2.5% yield in the mid-1990s would have earned a compound
average annual return of 12% a year for 15 years, he says.
Meanwhile, gold prices should resume a rally amid continued printing of money by the Fed and other central
banks, Mr. Prince says. Those efforts effectively devalue those countries' currencies compared with gold.
Mr. Prince also thinks stocks are attractive from a long-term perspective, especially compared with bonds or
cash. Broadly, discounted earnings-growth rates, which reflect the expectations about future earnings implied
by current prices, are negative, he says.
A moribund economic outlook "is pretty priced in right now," he says. "If we have a long, drawn out
deleveraging process without substantial air pockets, chances are equities are a pretty good bet, ironically."

Page 33 of 202 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Why Ron Paul Matters
Why Ron Paul Matters
By Edward H. Crane
1,105 words
31 December 2011
The Wall Street Journal
J
A15
English
Copyright 2011 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
The controversy surrounding decades-old newsletters to which GOP presidential aspirant Ron Paul lent his
name is regrettable. First, it is regrettable because the sometimes bigoted, intolerant content of those
newsletters is inconsistent with the views of the congressman as understood by those of us who know him.
Yet, while Mr. Paul disavows supporting those ideas, he refuses to repudiate his close association with their
likely source, Lew Rockwell, head of the Alabama-based Mises Institute.
Second, the New York Times editorialized recently that these unsavory writings "will leave a lasting stain on . .
. the libertarian movement." That is wishful thinking on the part of the Times, but it adds to the background
noise surrounding Mr. Paul's candidacy, obscuring the real libertarian policy initiatives that have made his
candidacy the most remarkable development of the 2012 campaign.
Ron Paul's libertarian campaign has traction because so many Americans respond to his messages:
-- Tax and spending. If ever there were sound and fury signifying nothing, it has to be the recent "debate"
over the budget. Covered by the media as though it was negotiations on the Treaty of Versailles, the
wrestling match between Republicans and Democrats centered on the nearly trivial question of whether the
$12 trillion increase in the national debt over the next decade should be reduced by 3% or 2%.
Mr. Paul would cut the federal budget by $1 trillion immediately. He can't do it, of course, but voters sense he
really wants to. As Milton Friedman once explained, the true tax on the American people is the level of
spending -- the resources taken from the private sector and employed in the public sector. Whether financed
from direct taxation, inflation or borrowing, spending is the burden.
-- Foreign policy and military spending. As the only candidate other than Jon Huntsman who says it is past
time to bring the troops home from Afghanistan, Mr. Paul has tapped into a stirring recognition by
limited-government Republicans and independents that an overreaching military presence around the world is
inconsistent with small, constitutional government at home.
The massive cost of these interventions, in treasure and blood, highlights what a mistake they are, as
sensible people on the left and right recognized from the beginning. Of course we want a strong military
capable of defending the United States, but our current expenditures equal what the rest of the world spends,
which makes little sense. It is futile to try to be the world's policeman -- to try to create an American Empire as
so many neoconservatives promote. And we can't afford it.
-- Civil liberties. Libertarians often differ with conservatives over issues related to civil liberties. Mr. Paul's
huge support among young people is due in large part to his fierce commitment to protecting the individual
liberties guaranteed us in the Constitution. He would work to repeal significant parts of the so-called Patriot
Act. Its many civil liberties transgressions include the issuance by the executive branch of National Security
Letters (a form of administrative subpoena) without a court order, and the forbiddance of American citizens
from mentioning that they have received one of these letters at the risk of jail.
The Bush and Obama administrations have claimed the right to incarcerate an American citizen on American
soil, without charge, without access to an attorney, for an indefinite period.
President Obama even claims the right to kill American citizens on foreign soil, without due process of law, for
suspected terrorist activities. Meanwhile, the Stop Online Piracy Act moving through the House is a clear
effort by the federal government to censor the Internet. Mr. Paul stands up against all this, which should and
does engender support from limited government advocates in the GOP.
-- Austrian economics. Mr. Paul is often criticized for references to what some consider obscure economists
of the so-called Austrian School. People should read them before criticizing. Nobel laureate Friedrich von
Hayek and his mentor Ludwig von Mises were two of the greatest economists and social scientists ever to
live.

--- Page 34 ---
Page 34 of 202 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Modern Austrian School economists such as Lawrence H. White, now at George Mason University, and Fred
Foldvary at Santa Clara University predicted the housing bubble and the recession that followed the massive,
multitrillion-dollar malinvestment caused by government redirection of capital into housing. Mr. Paul, like
Austrian School economists, understands that we would be better off with a gold standard, competing
currencies or a monetary rule than with the arbitrary and discretionary powers of our out-of-control Federal
Reserve.
Mr. Paul should be given credit for his efforts to promote these ideas and other libertarian policies, all of
which would make America better off. He'd be the first to admit he's not the most erudite candidate to make
the case, but surely part of his appeal is his very genuine persona.
Which is not to say that Mr. Paul is always in sync with mainstream libertarians. His seeming indifference to
attempts to prevent Iran from obtaining nuclear weapons, his support for a constitutional amendment to deny
birthright citizenship to children of illegal aliens, and his opposition to the Nafta and Cafta free trade
agreements in the name of doctrinal purity are at odds with most libertarians.
As for the Ron Paul newsletters, the best response was by my colleague David Boaz when the subject was
raised publicly in 2008. About them he wrote in the Cato Institute's blog:
"Those words are not libertarian words. Maybe they reflect 'paleoconservative' ideas, though they're not the
language of Burke or even Kirk. But libertarianism is a philosophy of individualism, tolerance, and liberty. As
Ayn Rand wrote, 'Racism is the lowest, most crudely primitive form of collectivism.' Making sweeping, bigoted
claims about all blacks, all homosexuals, or any other group is indeed a crudely primitive collectivism.
Libertarians should make it clear that the people who wrote those things are not our comrades, not part of our
movement, not part of the tradition of John Locke, Adam Smith, John Stuart Mill, William Lloyd Garrison,
Frederick Douglass, Ludwig von Mises, F. A. Hayek, Ayn Rand, Milton Friedman, and Robert Nozick. Shame
on them."
Support for dynamic market capitalism (as opposed to crony capitalism), social tolerance, and a healthy
skepticism of foreign military adventurism is a combination of views held by a plurality of Americans. It is why
the 21st century is likely to be a libertarian century. It is why the focus should be on Ron Paul's philosophy
and his policy proposals in 2012.
---
Mr. Crane is co-founder and president of the Cato Institute.

Retreat From Records --- After a strong start, this year was marked by declines in commodities prices
Retreat From Records --- After a strong start, this year was marked by declines in commodities prices
By Jerry A. DiColo
270 words
30 December 2011
The Wall Street Journal
J
C4
English
Copyright 2011 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
How the mighty have fallen.
The past year was dotted by records across commodities markets, as investors piled into the sector chasing
higher returns. But gains vanished in highflying markets.
Futures for gold and corn are both currently about 19% lower than their 2011 peaks, and copper and cotton
are ending the year in the red after their record runs.
There were plenty of reasons for the turnaround. Speculative investors were quick to close out positions as
soon as they detected that markets had topped out. And predictions of shortages of basic goods like cotton
and corn didn't come to pass. Meanwhile, the headwinds of a shaky recovery in the U.S. and Europe's debt
crisis contributed to the retreat as bullish investors worried about the decline of demand in a slowing global
economy.
In gold, exchanges acted to curtail runaway prices when futures neared $1,900 a troy ounce by raising
margins, or the amount of money investors need to put up to back futures contracts. Copper suffered from
diminished hopes for China's building boom and tightening credit in the world's second-largest economy.
Meanwhile, farmers responded to high prices by locking in profits on future production and planting more
crops, prompting traders to curtail their bullish bets.
For the coming year, with Europe's unresolved debt crisis, latent worries about inflation, uncertainty about
China's economic growth and the ultimate wildcard -- weather -- there is one confident prediction:
Commodities will keep investors on their toes.

Unease On Italy Ruffles Markets
Wednesday's Markets
Unease On Italy Ruffles Markets
By Jonathan Cheng
773 words
29 December 2011
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2011 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
The market's holiday from Europe's troubles lasted just a week.
After a brief respite, fresh concerns about the health of the euro zone reared their head again Wednesday,
driving the euro to a 14-month low and pushing the Standard & Poor's 500-stock index into negative territory
for the year.
The blue-chip Dow Jones Industrial Average suffered its worst loss in two weeks, falling 139.94 points, or
1.14%, to 12151.41. The measure is up 5% for 2011 with two trading days remaining in the year.
While traders were reluctant to read too much into the moves -- Wednesday was among the lightest trading
days this year -- the declines demonstrate that worries about Europe are rarely far from investors' minds.
Wednesday got off to a positive start as Italy successfully sold short-term debt, but thoughts soon turned to a
more-challenging sale Thursday of long-term bonds.
"People have gone from this successful auction to worrying about the next one," said John Fox, co-manager
of the FAM Value Fund, which manages $1.6 billion. "In the second half of the year, the only thing that's
mattered is Europe and we're back to that trade."
The euro's slide took it through $1.30 to $1.2941, the lowest since September 2010. Against the yen, the
common currency sank to 10-year lows.
Commodities were also savaged. Oil fell 2% and gold slumped to levels last seen in July. Silver tumbled
5.2% to an 11-month low.
Investors sought refuge in U.S. Treasurys, pushing the yield on the benchmark 10-year note back down
below 2% to 1.913%.
They also flocked to high-dividend-paying stocks, spurning bank stocks and commodities. All 30 components
of the Dow and all sectors of the S&P 500 finished lower.
The S&P 500, which lost 1.3% on Wednesday to snap a five-day winning streak, is off 0.6% for the year.
The selling was a shot of reality for investors who had hoped to finish a wild year on a quiet note. The
European Central Bank's move last week to extend three-year loans to 523 euro-zone banks had appeared
to calm many nerves, buoying those expectations.
Brian Jacobsen, chief portfolio strategist at Wells Fargo Funds Management, said that while Wednesday's
auction of short-term Italian debt was strong, it may have taken some buyers out of the market for Italy's more
important auction of 8.5 billion euros of 10-year notes slated for Thursday.
The long-term debt outlasts the three-year ECB loans, making it a better test of investor appetite for exposure
to European-government risk.
"People are hugging the shorter end of the yield curve," Mr. Jacobsen said. "There's a fear that the demand
for the shorter-dated securities is a substitute for demand for the longer-dated securities."
The concerns over the auction pushed yields for longer-term Italian debt higher throughout the U.S. morning.
Italy's 10-year yield finished at 6.971%, just off the 7% threshold that economists consider unsustainable for
fresh borrowings over the longer term.
European stock markets reversed their midday gains Wednesday to close broadly lower. The Stoxx Europe
600 finished down 0.7%, and Germany's DAX index gave up 2%.

Some investors warned that thin markets tend to exacerbate price movements during year-end trading. Just
2.4 billion shares changed hands in New York Stock Exchange composite volume, a touch above the two
billion shares traded on Tuesday -- the quietest full trading day of 2011.
Among the Dow industrials, Bank of America was the worst performer of the day, falling 20 cents, or 3.6%, to
$5.29, to bring its slide for the year to 60%.
New York Times slipped nine cents, or 1.2%, to 7.67 after the media company said it agreed to sell the 16
local newspapers that comprise its regional media group, for $143 million in cash, to Halifax Media Holdings.
Medicis Pharmaceutical fell 41 cents, or 1.2%, to 33.35 after lowering its revenue outlook.

day, while exporters in Japan were hit by the yen's surge against the euro.
Japan's Nikkei Stock Average lost 1.0%, Australia's S&P/ASX 200 dropped 1.2%, South Korea's Kospi
Composite fell 0.7% and New Zealand's NZX-50 eased 0.2%.
