Gold and silver rallied to five-week highs on the back of the European debt deal, as some traders seized on
aspects of the program they perceived as inflationary.
Gold futures for October delivery jumped $24, or 1.4%, to $1,746.70 a troy ounce on the Comex division of
the New York Mercantile Exchange. Silver surged $1.804, or 5.4%, to $35.095 an ounce. This is the highest
finish for both precious metals since the end of September.
One part of the European deal involves boosting a bailout fund by up to fivefold, to $1.4 trillion, and
recapitalizing banks.
George Gero, vice president at RBC Capital Markets Global Futures, said those moves could lead to higher
inflation if the injection of that money stimulates business activity.
"A bailout is a stimulus," Mr. Gero said. "The banks will have a more solid footing and will be more willing to
lend."
Precious metals tend to rise when inflation is a worry because many investors see them as a store of value.
That hasn't been the case recently, as the focus has stayed on sluggish growth and the overhang of Europe
on the global economy.
At one point in September, gold was down 16% from the $1,888.70 settlement record hit in August. That
plunge spooked some investors, and many switched over to cash when the threat of a credit crunch in
Europe loomed. For now, that shadow has been removed, which is also giving gold some impetus.
Silver's gains outpaced that of gold, as silver has dual roles as both a precious and industrial metal.
Silver's advance was augmented by upbeat economic data.

Europe Accord Is Boost
Wednesday's Markets
Europe Accord Is Boost
By Jonathan Cheng
450 words
27 October 2011
The Wall Street Journal
J
C5
English
Copyright 2011 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
The euro jumped to a seven-week high against the dollar and posted strong gains against the yen in early
Asian trading after European leaders reached an agreement on Greek debt and for expanding Europe's
emergency bailout fund.
The euro climbed to $1.3995 from a low of $1.3865 in early Asian trading and a late New York quote of
$1.3906. The common currency climbed to 106.44 yen from the New York quote of 105.98 yen. The dollar
was at 76.04 yen from 76.16 yen in New York. The Australian dollar, copper and oil rallied. Asian stocks were
moderately higher.
"Europe's debt has been one of the biggest headaches for many growth-sensitive assets, so the positive
development of the EU summit is a big morale booster," said a trader at SK Energy in Singapore.
In early trading, Japan's Nikkei Stock Average rose 0.5%, South Korea's Kospi Composite gained 1.0% and
Hong Kong's Hang Seng Index jumped 1.2%.
Some market participants remained skeptical about the European deal. "They are so good are presenting
things like this, but they've just bought some time and the debt issues are still far from over," said Masanobu
Ishikawa, a senior dealer at Tokyo Forex and Ueda Harlow.
In U.S. trading Wednesday, stocks finished a rocky day with strong gains, as investors grew optimistic about
European leaders' ability to contain the region's debt crisis, taking hope from the possibility of Chinese
investment in the euro-zone bailout fund.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average added 162.42 points, or 1.4%, to 11869.04, recouping most of the
previous day's losses. The Standard & Poor's 500-stock index gained 12.95 points, or 1.1%, to 1242.00,
while the Nasdaq Composite added 12.25 points, or 0.5%, to 2650.67.
The gains came after a volatile session that saw the Dow shoot up 162 points in the opening minutes of the
day, only to slide into negative territory. In the afternoon, stocks rebounded to the day's highs after reports
China would invest in Europe's bailout fund.
In overseas markets Wednesday, European stocks swung between gains and losses before finishing mixed
for the day. The Stoxx Europe 600 finished with a 0.2% gain, while Germany's DAX fell 0.5%.
Asian exchanges Wednesday erased early losses to trade mostly higher. China's Shanghai Composite added
0.7%.
Gold futures inched up to settle at $1,722.70 an ounce. Demand for safe-haven Treasurys fell, pushing the
yield on the benchmark 10-year up to 2.202%

The Vatican's Monetary Wisdom
The Vatican's Monetary Wisdom
By Robert A. Sirico
781 words
27 October 2011
The Wall Street Journal
J
A19
English
Copyright 2011 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
On Monday, the Vatican released an 18-page document titled "Toward Reforming the International Financial
and Monetary Systems in the Context of a Global Public Authority." Since then, it has been celebrated by
advocates of bigger government the world over.
What's ignored is that the document -- released to stimulate debate, not offer official doctrine -- embraces a
sound economic theory concerning the cause of the world financial crisis: the breakdown of the postwar
Bretton Woods monetary system and the unleashing of fiat currencies and central-bank printing presses.
Let's look at a representative passage, while keeping in mind several important markers: 1971 was the year
that the Nixon administration killed the gold standard, and along with it Bretton Woods and hard currencies;
in the early 1980s, financial deregulation in many countries removed the last major barriers to virtually
unlimited amounts of credit; and the 1990s was the decade when the drive to suppress interest rates
became the common policy of central banks around the world.
Since the 1990s, we have seen that money and credit instruments worldwide have grown more rapidly than
revenue, even adjusting for current prices. From this came the formation of pockets of excessive liquidity and
speculative bubbles which later turned into a series of solvency and confidence crises that have spread and
followed one another over the years.
A first crisis took place in the 1970s until the early 1980s and was related to the sudden sharp rises in oil
prices. Subsequently, there was a series of crises in the developing world, for example, the first crisis in
Mexico in the 1980s and those in Brazil, Russia and Korea, and then again in Mexico in the 1990s as well as
in Thailand and Argentina.
The speculative bubble in real estate and the recent financial crisis have the very same origin in the
excessive amount of money and the plethora of financial instruments globally.
This is sophisticated economic analysis. People are occupying Wall Street, blaming capitalism, speculation
and greed, but rare is the analysis that traces all these problems back to the structural change in money that
was brought about in the early 1970s.
We went from a hard-money regime, in which there were restrictions on the power of central banks and
financial institutions to create money and credit, to one where money became purely paper. There were no
restrictions remaining on the power of governments to finance unlimited debt. Banks could create credit
seemingly without limit. Central banks became the real power in the world economy.
None of this was true under a gold standard. That system limits the expansion of credit by an indelible
physical fact. There was a limit, a check, a rule that went beyond the whim of financial masters and
politicians. The Vatican seems to understand this.
But discerning the disease and finding the cure are very different undertakings, and here the document falls
short. It imagines a new world central bank and political authority that will rule without "any partial vision or
particular good" but rather seek "the common good." Its decisions should "be made in the interest of all, not
only to the advantage of some groups, whether they are formed by private lobbies or national governments."
Somehow, with an intelligence never before discovered in government bureaucracies, these proposed global
authorities would create "socio-economic, political and legal conditions essential for the existence of markets
that are efficient and efficacious."
Contrary to what is being said, this document presumes the existence and continuation of "free and stable
markets." The problem is that the Vatican imagines that a "world central bank" and a "global public authority"
can do this with more competence than national governments that have a checkered history in this regard.

--- Page 107 ---
Page 107 of 202 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
It was centralization that caused this mess in the first place. Central banks created paper money, easy and
limitless credit, and the moral hazard that accompanies them. Why should we believe that more centralization
is the solution when experience suggests precisely the opposite?
Many people who favor free markets worry about the implications of the Vatican document. And there is no
question that it will be used around the world to stir up political mischief. It will also be used to convince the
Catholic faithful that big-government solutions are morally justified. But let's not forget that there are really two
parts to the document: the diagnosis and the prescription. We should embrace the former and eschew the
latter.
---
Fr. Sirico is president of the Acton Institute in Grand Rapids, Mich.
(See related letter: "Letters to the Editor: Global Regulation Change Could Help" -- WSJ Novermber 8, 2011)

Breaking Chains: Gold, Stock Prices Part Ways
Commodities Report
Breaking Chains: Gold, Stock Prices Part Ways
By Tatyana Shumsky
447 words
26 October 2011
The Wall Street Journal
J
C4
English
Copyright 2011 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
NEW YORK -- Gold futures jumped 2.9% as the precious metal returned to its role as a safe haven amid
uncertainty over the coming European Union debt summit.
Gold futures briefly climbed above $1,700 a troy ounce for the first time in more than a month, while equity
and commodity prices fell. October gold later settled just shy of that level.
"This is a crucial day for gold," said Adam Klopfenstein, a senior market strategist with MF Global. "It's
moving up with bonds, the dollar and the yen, and that tells me the 'flight-to-quality' money is back in."
Investors traditionally expect gold to maintain its value during times of financial turmoil, but for the past two
months, the metal has risen and fallen in line with riskier assets such as stocks and commodities. Worries
that a euro-zone member was likely to default on its debt and create another global credit crunch had
investors selling almost everything to hold cash at the time.
Those concerns have receded as European governments have worked on a comprehensive plan that is likely
to include elements that are positive for gold.
The link between gold and other riskier assets finally snapped Tuesday, after European finance ministers
canceled a meeting set for the next day.
The cancellation unveiled simmering discord in the currency bloc. However, officials are wrangling over how
best to beef up the region's rescue fund, which could lead to more liquidity injections that would boost gold's
allure as a store of value. Bank recapitalizations have also been proposed which, if they occur, would mean
European lenders wouldn't need to sell gold to raise cash.
The contract for October delivery rose $48.10, or 2.9%, to settle at $1,699.60 a troy ounce, its highest
settlement since Sept. 22. The more actively traded December contract settled at $1,700.40.
Gold also got a boost Tuesday from falling Treasury-bond yields, which eased in response to the euro-zone
worries and a sharp decline in U.S. consumer confidence. "We've seen long-term bond yields fall, and that
reduced the opportunity cost of holding gold," said Bart Melek, senior commodity strategist with TD Securities.
The rest of the precious metals followed gold's cues, with October-delivery silver rallying $1.475, or 4.7%, to
settle at $33.095 a troy ounce. Nymex palladium for December delivery rose $13.60, or 2.1%, to settle at
$652.10 a troy ounce.
Platinum for January delivery gained $26.80, or 1.7%, to settle at $1,568.80 a troy ounce.

MCDONALD'S
Sales Gains Help Offset
Inflation in Food Costs
McDonald's Corp.'s third-quarter earnings rose 8.6% as the fast-food giant's continued sales gains helped
compensate for higher-than-expected food cost inflation.
Even after raising menu prices this year in light of its higher costs, the chain has still been able to boost
customer visits and grow sales faster than most of its competitors.
For the third quarter, global same-store sales, or sales at restaurants open at least 13 months, rose 5%, with
a 4.4% boost in the U.S., 4.9% in Europe and 3.4% in the Asia Pacific, Middle East and Africa division.
McDonald's reported a profit of $1.51 billion, or $1.45 a share, up from $1.39 billion, or $1.29 a share, a year
earlier. Foreign currency translation boosted its third quarter earnings by eight cents a share.
Revenue rose 14% from a year ago to $7.17 billion. Excluding currency fluctuations, revenue was up 8%.
-- Annie Gasparro

DAIMLER
Auto Maker Plans to Build
An All-New Model in U.S.
Daimler AG announced plans to build an all-new Mercedes-Benz model in the U.S., boosting investment in its
Tuscaloosa, Ala., plant by another $350 million and expanding its manufacturing footprint in North America.
The German luxury car maker declined to disclose or describe the new model, which it will begin producing in
Tuscaloosa in 2015, but said it would be an "entirely new" product aimed at boosting Mercedes sales,
particularly in the U.S.
"This new model from the Tuscaloosa plant is an important element of our growth strategy," said Daimler
Chief Executive Dieter Zetsche in a statement. The addition of the new Mercedes model would bring 400 jobs
and increase Daimler's total planned investment in its Tuscaloosa plant between 2010 and 2014 to nearly
$2.4 billion. The auto maker already makes its M-, GL- and R-Class luxury sports utility vehicles at the
Alabama plant. It also has plans to begin producing C-Class cars for the North American market in Alabama
as of 2014.

U.S. mining giant Freeport-McMoRan Copper & Gold Inc. said unknown gunmen killed three people Friday
near its mine in eastern Indonesia, where thousands of workers are on strike to demand higher pay.
Also Friday, the two sides returned to the negotiating table. Union spokesman Juli Parorongan said the local
government had given them 24 hours to settle their differences, but that it was unlikely an agreement would
be reached so quickly.
Ninety percent of the Grasburg mine's 12,000 unionized workers walked off their jobs on Sept. 15, demanding
that current salaries of between $2.10 to $3.50 an hour be pushed to as high as $7.50 to $43 an hour.
They've since backed down a bit, asking for up to $33, but that's still well above what the Phoenix,
Ariz.-based company is willing to pay.
Violence near the mine has flared several times in the last week. One contract worker and two nonemployees
were shot by unknown gunmen early Friday, said company spokesman Ramdani Sirait, adding that police
were investigating the incident with the company's full support and cooperation.
Five Freeport workers were killed in recent weeks -- two by police trying to control a crowd and three by
unknown gunmen.
-- Associated Press

SARA LEE
Divestiture Clears Way
For Sale to Mexico's Bimbo
Sara Lee Corp. on Friday reached a divestiture agreement with the U.S. Justice Department that will clear the
way for the sale of its North American fresh bakery business to Mexico's Grupo Bimbo SAB.
With the move, Sara Lee lowered the unit's selling price to $709 million from $959 million, which includes
assumed debt.
The proposed settlement, which still requires approval by a federal judge, would force Grupo Bimbo's BBU
unit to divest the rights to sell certain brands in California, Kansas, Oklahoma City, Nebraska and central
Pennsylvania after it acquires the Sara Lee breads unit.
Sara Lee repeated its plan to declare a $3 per-share special dividend, though the timing of the payout still
hasn't been determined.
Under the proposed transaction, Sara Lee will retain the right to sell Sara Lee branded frozen desserts and
meat products, including sliced deli meats. BBU will have the rights to the Sara Lee brand in the fresh baked
goods category globally, except for Australia and New Zealand.

OFF DUTY --- Adventure & Travel -- School Trip: Fall For Food Vacations --- Bring home cooking smarts from five top kitchen retreats
OFF DUTY --- Adventure & Travel -- School Trip: Fall For Food Vacations --- Bring home cooking
smarts from five top kitchen retreats
By Elizabeth Gunnison
611 words
22 October 2011
The Wall Street Journal
J
D10
English
Copyright 2011 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Back-to-school season coincides with harvest time in the cooler climes, when markets are full of fresh, ripe
bounty. It's a perfect for excursions that teach you a culinary lesson or two. From getting a butchery education
in the quiet of Upstate New York to eating across India's Rajasthan in style, here are some of the best food
getaways for this autumn and winter.
WINE
A Grape Education at Blackberry Farm
In mid-November the five-star hideaway will host its annual Wine Geek 101 event, three days focused on the
ins and outs of tasting, choosing and pairing wine, in the foothills of Tennessee's Smoky Mountains. This
year, Master Sommelier Andrea Immer, one of the country's leading wine educators and writers, will share
her grape knowledge in seminars, lunches and dinners at the luxury property and organic farm. During free
afternoons, guests can indulge in Blackberry Farm's other culinary activities, including cooking
demonstrations and tours of the farm and gardens.
$1,200 per person, not including lodging; Nov. 13-16; blackberryfarm.com
HEALTHY WAYS
Create Spa Cuisine Where it Was Born
And you thought you were ahead of the trends. Rancho La Puerta, on the U.S.-Mexico border, has espoused
the wisdom of eating local, minimally processed food since 1940. Visiting instructors teach classes each
week; French food expert Patricia Wells will be in residence the week of Nov. 26. Fill the rest of your days
helping head gardener Salvador Tinajero work the resort's six-acre organic farm, or choose from dozens of
fitness activities and indulgent spa treatments.
Classes from $75 each; one-week stay from $2,835 per person; rancholapuerta.com
BAKING
Bread in Bath
Picturesque Bath, England, is home to Richard Bertinet's five-day course, which enthusiasts say will
revolutionize the way you think about bread. The James Beard Award-winning chef and author of the
acclaimed books "Dough" and "Crust" personally leads the hands-on program, which runs the gamut from
French breads to Italian, "dark and sweet" loaves to sourdoughs. Classes top out at 12 students and can fill
up months in advance. One- and three-day options are also available.
From about $300 per day; space still available Jan. 16-20, 2012; thebertinetkitchen.com
SOUTH ASIAN COOKING
Eat Your Way Around India
Peggy Markel has long received high marks for her intimate culinary trips around the Mediterranean and
North Africa. This winter she leads food lovers into India for 12 days. The Rajasthan tour will feature meals at
some of the region's finest restaurants, market visits, interactions with local craftsmen and farmers, and
private cooking classes and demonstrations. Guests benefit from Ms. Markel's relationships with local estate
owners and families.
$9,950 per person for a double-occupancy room, not including airfare; Feb. 5-17, 2012; peggymarkel.com

--- Page 114 ---
Page 114 of 202 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
BUTCHERING
Learn a Little (Or a Lot) About Meat
Hudson Valley-based Fleisher's Grass-Fed & Organic Meats has become the gold standard not just for
artisan meat products, but for butcher education. Husband-and-wife team Jessica and Joshua Applestone
offer an intensive 101 course for amateurs seeking in-depth knowledge of sustainable meat cleaving. Five
days at the couple's Kingston, N.Y., facility are spent on knife skills, safety and anatomy lessons; sausage
making, beef and chicken demonstrations; and lamb and pig carving workshops. For the milder meat
enthusiast, there are also one-day classes.
$2,100 for five days, not including lodging; next available Nov. 6-10; fleishers.com

REVIEW --- Colorful Coffins and Funerals to Die For --- What explains our constantly evolving rituals for the dead?
REVIEW --- Colorful Coffins and Funerals to Die For --- What explains our constantly evolving rituals
for the dead?
By Sarah Murray
983 words
22 October 2011
The Wall Street Journal
J
C3
English
Copyright 2011 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Within hours of the death of Steve Jobs earlier this month, impromptu memorials began to appear at Apple
stores around the world. Well-wishers brought candles, flowers and apples (with bites taken from them), and
they covered windowpanes with handwritten Post-It notes. Inventive and colorful, such outpourings are this
generation's way of memorializing public figures like Princess Diana and Alexander McQueen.
But creativity in sending off the dead is nothing new. Human beings are seemingly hardwired to use public
rituals to mark such moments. We bury our loved ones in the ground. We burn them in fire. In some cultures,
we leave corpses as carrion, inviting the birds to pick the bones dry. In others, we hang the dead in trees or
stow them in caves. In naval circles, we consign them to the ocean.
I had my own epiphany about death rituals on a trip to Bali. In the small town of Ubud, with tropical birdsong
in the air and ornate Hindu temples on every corner, I encountered a giant bull. There it stood, at least 15 feet
high, planted firmly on an ornate plinth. This spectacular beast was made of paper, wood and bamboo.
Painted in black, red and gold, its mane sparkled with tiny mirrors, and strings of beads adorned its neck.
This majestic bull was a sarcophagus that would -- with the assistance of a giant pyre -- take the spirit of
Agung Suyasa, a Balinese prince, on his journey to the next life. A few days later, I watched as the bull and
its human cargo were consumed by fire. The ceremonial bovine soon became a black silhouette amid a
raging conflagration. Watching this extraordinary ceremony surrounded by demons, dragons and princesses
(and the princesses were real), I felt I'd wandered into a fairy tale. And it was all for the benefit of a mortal
who had reached his expiration date.
In Ghana, an unusual burial tradition started in the 1950s, so the story goes, when a tribal chief
commissioned a giant wooden cocoa pod as a ceremonial litter to be carried on poles. The chief died before
this palanquin was completed, so it was used for his funeral. Inspired by the chief's unusual coffin, a furniture
maker called Seth Kane Kwei created a casket for his grandmother to fulfill her unrealized dream to ride in an
airplane.
Today, gigantic fish, oversized mobile phones, scaled-down airplanes and enormous Coca-Cola bottles are
among the fantasy coffins on offer. Boldly carved and painted in bright colors, they are works of art. The
exuberance also extends to Ghanaian funerals, which often attract hundreds of guests who are fed, watered
and entertained with live music, drumming and DJs.
In some cultures, the party continues well after the dead have drawn their last breath. In Madagascar, the
Merina community take their deceased from their resting places every few years and wrap their bones in silk.
Families eat, drink and dance, with their ancestors hoisted up on their shoulders, occasionally tossing them
exuberantly up into the air before returning them to their tombs.
Dancing and death also unite in Mexico during the Day of the Dead festival in early November. People
dressed as skeletons, devils and monsters cavort through the streets to the music of small brass bands.
Mexicans like to feed the spirits of the departed, too. Ofrendas -- elaborately decorated shrines erected in
homes, shops and offices -- are piled high with sugar skulls, corpse-shaped loaves of bread, glasses of water
(the dead are always thirsty) and other food items.
These vividly varied ceremonial traditions demonstrate our strong need for a continuing connection with the
dead. They also serve as a means of persuading ourselves that there is indeed some form of life after death.
Earlier generations had no doubt, of course, about the existence of an afterlife. In 17th-century America,
primers and prayer books exhorted readers to be in a state of constant preparation -- and anxiety -- for the
moment when their lives would be weighed in the balance.

--- Page 116 ---
Page 116 of 202 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Such foreboding about divine judgment is much less common now. At modern funerals, the eulogy -- in which
friends and family reflect on the character of the deceased -- takes center stage. Today, we tend to devote
more time to remembering the lives of the dead than to praying for their souls.
As we have become less certain of spiritual eternity, we also have started to look for solace in the more
tangible things of this world. More of us want to hold on to physical evidence of our dead loved ones -- in
lockets designed to house tiny portions of their ashes or in gold pendants that immortalize their thumbprints,
keepsakes marketed as "Thumbies" by Illinois-based Meadow Hill. LifeGem, a Chicago-based company,
uses carbon extracted from cremated remains to make diamonds.
We also are growing more creative in how we send off the departed. Hot Rod Caskets, based in Oklahoma,
designs and produces a range of flashy models, including the Smoke Eater (with attachments for pike poles)
for firefighters and the Uncle Sam (in desert sand and olive green, with camouflage lining) for members of the
military. For sports fans, Michigan-based Eternal Image produces coffins decked out in the colors and logos
of Major League Baseball teams, as well as a Star Trek line for Trekkies.
In England, Nottingham-based Vic Fearn & Company, which has been making coffins since the 1860s, now
offers customized designs. Recent coffins have included a river barge, a giant guitar, a vintage Rolls-Royce
and a huge egg. They are decorative, celebratory and highly personal. The Balinese and the Ghanaians
surely would approve.
---
Ms. Murray is the author of "Making an Exit: From the Magnificent to the Macabre -- How We Dignify the
Dead."

'Beige Book' Casts Pall Over the Blue Chips
Wednesday's Markets
'Beige Book' Casts Pall Over the Blue Chips
By Jonathan Cheng
347 words
20 October 2011
The Wall Street Journal
J
C4
English
Copyright 2011 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Stocks dropped as a gloomy assessment of the U.S. economy from the Federal Reserve added to a sharp
fall in technology stocks after Apple's earnings disappointment.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average fell 72.43 points, or 0.6%, to finish at 11504.62. The Standard & Poor's
500-stock index lost 15.50 points, or 1.3%, to 1209.88, and the Nasdaq Composite shed 53.39 points, or 2%,
to 2604.04.
The Dow spent much of the day in positive territory but quickly sank after the release of the Fed's "beige
book" report of domestic economic activity, as investors pared back risk in line with the central bank's
cautious take on the economy.
Weighing heavily on the markets were technology stocks, after Apple's fiscal fourth-quarter earnings and
revenue fell short of expectations. The stock slumped $23.62 a share, or 5.6%, to $398.62 after finishing at
an all-time high Tuesday.
Hewlett-Packard lost 63 cents, or 2.5%, to 24.98, and Cisco Systems fell 35 cents, or 2%, to 17.16.
In Europe, the Stoxx Europe 600 advanced 0.6%.

Composite added 0.3% and New Zealand's NZX-50 fell 0.3%.

Short-Term Lenders Seize the Day --- Shares Rise for Pawn Shops and Firms Offering Payday Loans as More Borrowers Head Their Way
Short-Term Lenders Seize the Day --- Shares Rise for Pawn Shops and Firms Offering Payday Loans
as More Borrowers Head Their Way
By Lynn Cowan and Isabel Ordonez
668 words
19 October 2011
The Wall Street Journal
J
C4
English
Copyright 2011 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Tough times are good times for payday lenders and pawn shops.
They are taking advantage of anemic economic growth, banks' unwillingness to lend and regulatory
dysfunction to thrive.
Borrowers on the fringes of the financial system are gravitating to these companies, known as payday lenders
because they offer short-term loans to borrowers who often need tiding over between paychecks. Pawn
shops offer similar loans, but require valuables such as jewelry or high-end electronics as collateral; those
items can later be sold by the shops under certain circumstances. The companies are pursuing a group of
customers that bigger banks, focused more on improving the quality of their borrowers, are avoiding.
Shares of nationwide chains, such as DFC Global Corp., Cash America International Inc. and First Cash
Financial Services Inc., have jumped to records in recent weeks. Meanwhile, plans for two initial public
offerings from the industry, including the online business of Cash America, have been filed since August.
Cash America representatives declined to comment. DFC Global and First Cash representatives didn't return
calls seeking comment.
Though share prices have come off their highs in recent weeks, they are still up for the year. Cash America is
up 49%, First Cash Financial 40% and DFC Global 18%. That compares with the KBW Bank Index, which is
down 26% so far in 2011.
First Cash Financial is set to report third-quarter earnings Wednesday, with other payday lenders and pawn
shops also reporting this week and next. Analysts covering DFC, Cash America and First Cash indicate they
expect earnings at all of the companies to increase by double-digit percentages in the third quarter, though it
is possible earnings could disappoint. All three beat expectations in the second quarter.
In a Cash America store near downtown Houston, 48-year-old George Espinosa was looking to pawn a gold
necklace for a 60-day loan of $350 in order to pay some bills. His take-home pay as a delivery driver is being
squeezed by this summer's jump in gasoline prices. Mr. Espinosa said he hocked last week a driving
navigation system for $35 at 24% interest. He has three months to pay the loan.
People like pawn shops because they can get loans without references or credit checked, Mr. Espinosa says.
"You just bring something, and they give you the loan right away," he says.
Loan terms are usually in weeks, compared with the months and years offered by banks, and the principal is
relatively small. But the interest rates can be in the triple digits. In Texas, a person today taking a $500 loan
from Cash America would have to repay $602.29 in two weeks. That is an annual percentage rate of more
than 533%. In contrast, Citigroup Inc.'s Citibank unit offers personal loans to qualified borrowers at APRs
ranging from 10.49% to 25.49% over a period of 24 to 60 months.
In their IPO filings, Community Choice Financial Inc., which operates check-cashing and consumer-loan
stores in 14 states, and Enova International Inc., an online lender being carved out of parent Cash America,
both warn that new regulations could lead to slower growth and lower profits. For now, though, despite the
lobbying of consumer groups, it is the states, not the federal government, that are the biggest threats. Several
states have laws capping interest rates, while the Dodd-Frank financial overhaul doesn't allow the recently
created Consumer Financial Protection Bureau to set caps on how much interest these companies can
charge.
While the agency could pursue other measures regulating payday lenders, that is only likely to happen once a
permanent director is in place. President Barack Obama's pick must be confirmed by the Senate, and
Republicans there have vowed to block any nominee until the agency's structure is revamped.

Stocks Fend Off Bear, Up For Year
Stocks Fend Off Bear, Up For Year
By Jonathan Cheng and Brendan Conway
1,020 words
15 October 2011
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
Copyright 2011 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
In less than two weeks, U.S. stocks have staged a remarkable recovery that saw major indexes climb from
the depths of near-bear-market despair to positive territory for the year.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average on Friday climbed 166.36 points, or 1.45%, to 11644.49, taking its gains
since early last week to 12%. It is also now up 0.58% for the year.
Over the past 10 months, investors have weathered a debt downgrade of the U.S. government, an escalating
debt crisis in Europe, political upheaval in the Middle East and disaster in Japan. But by Friday, it felt like old
news.
Nine trading days ago, investors were standing on the edge of a bear market, by definition. The Standard &
Poor's 500-stock index was down more than 20% from its April high and then the rally began.
While no single factor was seen as propelling recent gains, investors cited signs of progress in reaching a
resolution to Europe's debt crisis, as well as improving U.S. economic data.
On Friday, the U.S. economy showed rare signs of strength. Retail sales rose 1.1% in September, the biggest
gain since February. And a positive earnings report from Google Inc. helped drive the rally.
While many investors remained wary, especially in Europe, the sheer power of the recent rally has prompted
many others to join.
"There are so many people who were out of the market who are now saying, 'Gee, I just missed a 10%
move,' " said Jenkins Marshall, managing director at Knight Capital, a trading firm in Jersey City, NJ. "And, 'I
can't afford to miss the next 10% move.' "
Major indexes have risen for three weeks in a row, their longest streak in six months. This week, the Dow
gained 4.9%, while the technology-focused Nasdaq Composite surged 7.6%, its biggest weekly gain since
March 2009, as Apple Inc., International Business Machines Corp. and Amazon.com Inc. jumped to record
highs.
Google's better-than-expected earnings, as well as the arrival of the newest version of the iPhone in retail
stores, helped power the rally in technology stocks. Investors were also growing more optimistic about
earnings for Apple and IBM, which both report in the coming week.
The week's stock gains were accompanied by a decline in the dollar, while the euro enjoyed its biggest gain
since March 2009, rising to its highest level in a month on Friday. The lower dollar, combined with optimism
about the global economy, also helped boost commodity prices.
Gold and silver each rose by 2.9% or more this week, and copper -- an industrial metal seen as a bellwether
for global economic growth -- added 4.2% for its best week in three months. Crude-oil futures, meantime,
climbed 4.6% this week, its biggest such gain since April.
The relative strength in the U.S. stock market is in contrast to the rest of the world, where almost every other
major stock index, from Sao Paulo to Copenhagen, is down sharply for the year. Venezuela's market is the
only other major stock index in positive territory for 2011.
"You've got to put your money somewhere, and you've got to go with the U.S.," said Rick Fier, vice president
of equity trading at Conifer Securities, with $12 billion under management. "After a week like this, people are
starting to get scared that it's like last year, where you get a rip-roaring rally in the fourth quarter that leaves
you behind."

--- Page 121 ---
Page 121 of 202 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Still, investors face a daunting landscape. The U.S. economy is far from healthy and concerns have been
growing that inflation pressures could weigh on developing economies.
The biggest challenge is Europe. France and Germany moved toward a three-part agreement Friday to help
stem the crisis: a new bailout for Greece, measures to protect the region's banks and providing additional
strength for a broader bailout fund.
The agreement came from a meeting in Paris of finance ministers from the Group of 20 industrialized and
developing nations.
"European leaders finally appear to recognize the risks of inaction," said Timothy McCandless, senior equity
analyst at Bel Air Investment Advisors. Still, Mr. McCandless said, the market may soon move from wanting
improved rhetoric from Europe toward "wanting to see specific action."
Much of the market's moves in recent weeks have been the result of Europe news, as leaders squabbled
over plans for helping bail out Greece. That has led to unprecedented volatility in the stock market, in the U.S.
in particular, and sent investors from small traders to big hedge funds fleeing the market.
Friday's move was the 57th time in 58 trading days that the Dow has moved 100 points or more during the
day.
Those types of moves have in large part made investors skeptical of sudden gains. And this week's rise was
particularly curious because of the scarcity of U.S. economic data. Trading activity, as well, has been low. On
Friday, New York Stock Exchange composite volume was 3.6 billion shares, well below the daily average this
year of 4.4 billion and the lowest in six weeks.
Analysts typically like to see high levels of trading before they are convinced that a rally is for real. Bank of
AmericaMerrill Lynch analyst Mary Ann Bartels said earlier this week that the gains were more likely driven by
investors taking off their negative bets on stocks, "which is not the recipe for a sustainable rally."
Some analysts believe the overall stock market is in the midst of a much-longer downturn and any rally now,
while potentially powerful, may not be long lasting.
Technical analysts, who study stock charts for clues about the movement of the markets, said stocks were
due for a rebound after falling toward bear-market lows the prior week. Still, they remain divided on whether
stocks can move higher.

Big Developer Bets on Queens
Big Developer Bets on Queens
By Laura Kusisto
1,012 words
10 October 2011
The Wall Street Journal
J
A21
English
Copyright 2011 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
The LeFrak family, which built thousands of apartment units in Queens in the 1960s and 1970s, will create its
first new residential project in the borough in 38 years.
The Manhattan real-estate family's third-generation head, Richard LeFrak, plans to convert a 13-story office
tower in Rego Park built by his father 50 years ago into 108 luxury rentals. The project is tiny compared with
the family's megadevelopments in the region, such as LeFrak City in Queens and the Newport complex in
Jersey City.
The Queens project reflects a new era of real-estate investment in that borough. When the family built LeFrak
City, it was manufacturing low- and middle-income housing for teachers, firefighters and the area's working
class. Now, the LeFraks are planning to build high-end rentals to cater to Queens' booming young and
immigrant population.
At the same time, the Rego Park conversion is a sign of what may be an important shift in one of the city's
storied real-estate families, which famously thumbed its nose at New York in the 1980s and focused its
real-estate investments on Newport and other areas. The head of the family at the time, Samuel LeFrak, said
he was fed up with bureaucracy and the high cost of development in the city. The LeFraks haven't developed
anything in the city for 28 years.
Richard LeFrak, 66 years old, said his decision to create a new rental project in Queens reflects renewed
confidence in the borough's growing population, easy access to transportation and low vacancy rates. Mr.
LeFrak says he'll be watching how the Rego Park project fares when deciding whether to pursue other
projects in the area.
"I've had other people offer me opportunities," he said. But for now he said the cost of city development sites
is too high.
While the family may be once again eyeing New York projects, Newport remains a higher priority. The
400-acre project along the New Jersey waterfront already includes about 20 residential, commercial and hotel
buildings, with more than 5,000 families and 20,000 office employees.
Newport is about halfway complete, and the LeFraks plan to announce the next building shortly. Richard
LeFrak has a three-dimensional model of what has become known as the Gold Coast in his lobby and
frequently takes visitors on guided tours.
The family also is eyeing future development in the region at a time that a new generation is beginning to take
a larger role in the company's leadership. Richard's two sons, James and Harrison, have taken executive
roles in the company, and in the last few years have taken lead roles in a number of investments in
out-of-state and international real estate, as well as more complex investments in banking and distressed
assets.
Both brothers say they remain committed to the region. Harrison, 39, emphasized that the company has
always been rooted in New York City. "We continue to focus on the New York market, which is a place that
amazing, talented and brilliant people have wanted to be for a long time," he said in an interview.
But the brothers echo their father and grandfather's frustration with the high price of development sites and
convoluted New York City bureaucracy. Local politicians "seem to abhor the private ownership of property,"
said James, 37.
While most of the city's real-estate families, such as the Dursts and Rudins, got their start in Manhattan, the
LeFraks began building their empire in the other boroughs. The family patriarch, Harry LeFrak, immigrated to
the U.S. from Palestine around the turn of the last century and developed his first houses on a farm he

--- Page 123 ---
Page 123 of 202 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
bought in Williamsburg in 1916, according to the book "Skyscraper Dreams, the Great Real Estate Dynasties
of New York."
Harry LeFrak's only son, Samuel, greatly expanded the real-estate business, developing LeFrak City, a
42-acre middle-income complex with 5,000 apartments in Forest Hills, Queens. He also became a
well-known and feisty New York character who was a master at using the press to get attention.
Richard LeFrak took over as president in 1975, and played a leading role in the Newport development, built
on derelict rail yards with Simon Property Group, the largest mall owner in the country. Work started in 1985
and it was supposed to be finished in 20 years. But the development ran into delays and other problems
during the recession of the early 1990s.
"We've weathered a lot of tough times on that deal," said David Simon, chief executive of Simon Property.
"But that development really created the Gold Coast up and down Hoboken. It took a lot of vision to do it, a
lot of guts and a lot of money."
The LeFraks have survived the latest downturn virtually unscathed. During the boom years, they avoided
top-of-the-market investments and they have kept their debt level low. Indeed, they've been focused lately on
taking advantage of distressed situations. In 2009, for example, the LeFraks joined a group of investors led
by Starwood that purchased $4.5 billion of defaulted condo loans made by Corus Bank after Corus was
seized by the Federal Deposit Insurance Corporation.
While other New York real-estate families have run into problems with succession, the LeFraks say they are
avoiding them. When asked whether James or Harrison LeFrak will run the company in the future, the
brothers say the question is premature. "With every succeeding generation," Harrison said, "the father-son
relationship has been refined and improved. Our family has gotten better and our business has gotten better."
Executives who have worked with the LeFraks say the sons complement each other's roles.
"It's like Richard was split in half. You have the creative out-of-the-box thinker in Harry, and the nuts and
bolts, knows the business, sweats the details in Jamie," said Barry Sternlicht, CEO of Starwood Capital.

Europe and Its Money
REVIEW & OUTLOOK (Editorial)
Europe and Its Money
1,442 words
8 October 2011
The Wall Street Journal
J
A14
English
Copyright 2011 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
The German Bundestag voted last week to expand the European fund established last year to bail out
Greece -- or, rather, Greece's creditors. In doing so, it also moved the euro zone one step further away from
the bloc's founding principle that its members would share a currency but be responsible for their own fiscal
policies within that currency zone.
---
In the current global environment of floating exchange rates, this idea seemed radical, and many people
thought the experiment was doomed to failure. Yet historically speaking, the real novelty is our system of fiat
currencies and floating exchange rates. The modern monetary era began 40 years ago, when President
Richard Nixon closed the gold window. That act led to the collapse of the postwar Bretton Woods system, of
which the U.S. dollar, convertible to gold at $35 an ounce, was the anchor.
The end of Bretton Woods led to floating exchange rates and a decade of inflation. For the first time in
history, the entire global economy rested on paper currencies with no formal link to precious metals or, for the
most part, to each other.
It's true that countries had gone off the gold standard before: sometimes frequently, and most often because
of war, which would drive spending beyond a nation's ability to pay its bills in a hard currency. But these
moves had always been temporary. What happened after 1971 was different, and it's hard to argue that the
results have been stellar. The past 40 years have seen inflation, government spending and government debt
all rise significantly in much of the West.
The euro, in this context, was less a leap into the unknown than an attempt to return to an older discipline,
one in which governments would not rely on plenipotentiary central banks to bail them out of their policy
errors. Europe was attempting a return to a hard currency in which states were expected to pay their way, or
pay the consequences.
Today the euro's critics see vindication in Greece's continuing collapse and the turmoil that it is causing in
euroland. But if the euro unravels -- a possibility that can no longer be ruled out -- it will be because, when the
test of its founding principles came amid this crisis, those principles were abandoned.
Joining the euro meant giving up independent monetary policy. The single currency's critics argue that this
is destabilizing, that it will lead to interest rates being too high in one country and too low in another, and so
on. This objection would have more force if independent central banks had a better track record of getting
monetary policy right even in one country. But they don't.
The better question is whether the things that an independent monetary policy allows you to do are worth
doing.
If the government controls the central bank, it can try to manipulate the supply and price of credit through
open-market operations. But as we can see in Europe, Japan and the U.S. today, even near-zero or negative
real interest rates do not guarantee robust growth or full employment. It's surprising to see the degree to
which self-described free-marketeers abandon their skepticism of government omniscience when it comes to
the intractable problem of the right price of credit in an economy.
Next, a central bank can print money to help the government meet its spending plans without immediately
raising taxes. But over time, this will drive up inflation to intolerable levels as the oversupply of money
destroys its value. Think Weimar Germany or modern Zimbabwe before it dollarized its economy. Even in
milder forms, as a kind of Keynesian stimulus, this facilitation of deficit spending can provide only a short-term
economic boost.

--- Page 125 ---
Page 125 of 202 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
The central bank can also try to manipulate the supply of money in circulation to drive up or (more often)
down the foreign-exchange value of its currency, to the short-term advantage of its exporters and the
detriment of those who depend on imports. This is the preferred remedy for those who believe that Greece's
salvation lies in exiting the euro, re-adopting the drachma, and promptly devaluing it.
In reality, this means making Greeks poorer by reducing the value of their assets and increasing the price of
everything the country imports. In exchange, devaluation will make Greek exports, such as they are, cheaper
on international markets. That might flatter Greece's economic statistics for a time, but at the price of an
enormous destruction of savings and wealth. Economists call this "beggaring thy neighbor," but it might better
be thought of as beggaring thyself.
That the euro has denied its members the opportunities to perform these acts of economic vandalism is
nothing to lament. These are not problems in the design of the euro. They were the objective.
The architects of the euro did recognize the serious potential problem with a free rider like Greece. Markets
would price its debt as if the country would be bailed out, and so market signals like bond prices would be
muted in a currency zone until it was too late. That's why Europe tried to establish a mechanism, the Stability
and Growth Pact, to constrain governments within the bloc.
But these rules were never enforced. Nobel economist Robert Mundell argued on these pages in 1998 that
sanctions for breaching the deficit rules should be automatic, and that if they were subject to majority voting
they would be gamed, as in fact they were.
The euro has thus obviously not been a perfect cure for governmental incontinence, but it has been a
reasonably stable unit of account for the Continent for a decade. It could be once again -- if the principle
could be re-established that when a country can't pay its bills, its creditors will take the losses.
If that can't be done, then we will get either a fiscal union that merges national budget policies, or dissolution.
The conceit is that fiscal union will, over time, make Europe more like Germany than Greece. But in practice
the opposite is more likely, as the political pressure will always be for higher taxes, a little more inflation, a
little more stimulus and increased transfers from rich countries to poor, or from successful economic models
to the less successful.
Alternatively the currency bloc could break up. If Greece leaves, it would establish a precedent that countries
in trouble will exit the bloc. The euro is a pure fiat currency without a fiscal authority behind it, and the
perception of permanence is the only thing keeping it together so far. The costs of breakup would also be
extremely high, as markets discount the chances that others will leave and recalibrate political and economic
risks across the bloc.
The better solution would be to return to the euro's original principles, this time with a system that can
convince markets that there is genuine discipline. The various funds and ECB bond-buying plans are not
such discipline. They may buy short-term confidence, but they will not assure lenders or taxpayers that the
same cycle won't repeat itself.
The first step toward imposing such discipline would be a Greek default with haircuts for creditors. This would
allow Athens to reduce its debt burden and once again have the capacity to grow, assuming it changes its
policies. This will be painful for Greece and the banks that lent to Greece, but not nearly as painful as a
breakup of the euro zone or more years of continuing the current bailout patchwork.
---
The Greek crisis hasn't proved that the euro was a mistake. But the political reaction to it has placed the
experiment in serious danger by abandoning the no-bailout principle and replacing it with a doctrine that
sovereign default in a currency zone is unthinkable. This policy makes no more sense than saying that
bankruptcy should be impossible, because no one will lend to a company if it might go bust. What's worse is
that Europe's leadership has adopted this view for the sake of defending the Greek government from the
consequences of its irresponsibility on spending and mendacity about its true level of deficit and debt.
The solution is not a return to monetary "sovereignty." Countries don't need unfettered monetary freedom.
Only governments do, to bail themselves out of their own bad policies. If the euro should fail, it will be
because those governments did too little to hold themselves to the discipline that a hard currency, albeit a
paper one, demanded.
Document J000000020111008e7a800023

--- Page 126 ---
Page 126 of 202 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Reason to Pull REITs from the Wreckage
Heard on the Street
Reason to Pull REITs from the Wreckage
By John Jannarone
454 words
6 October 2011
The Wall Street Journal
J
C16
English
Copyright 2011 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
[Financial Analysis and Commentary]
What will investors abandon next? The first bout of jitters in July hit stocks. Then gold took its licks. The latest
target: companies that own government-backed mortgages.
After holding up well through the summer, many mortgage real-estate investment trusts have fallen at least
15% in the last month. Such REITs tend to perform better when investors flee risky assets because they
invest exclusively in government-agency-guaranteed mortgages. But Capstead Mortgage Corp. and CYS
Investments now have valuations below 90% of book value, the lowest in over a year, estimates Steve
DeLaney of JMP Securities.
One big concern: REITs use short-term borrowing arrangements called repos, or repurchase agreements, to
leverage their purchases of the low-yielding securities. That leverage helps boost their dividend payouts,
which in some cases lead to yields of about 20%.
Some investors have questioned that strategy in light of the banking crisis in Europe, which could cause
lenders to pull back. But it is important to remember that government-backed mortgages are among the
safest collateral and the repo market made it through the 2008 crisis.
The more immediate worry is that homeowners will refinance their mortgages if the Federal Reserve's plan to
reduce long-term rates has legs. That would hurt REITs because in some cases they have bought mortgages
at a premium to their face value, but would only receive par on their investments if homeowners refinance
early.
That threat alone doesn't look serious enough to justify current REIT valuations. Currently, REITs earn about
two percentage points of spread between their borrowing costs and the income from mortgage securities.
With leverage of up to 10 times, that return is amplified to 20%. Conservatively assuming homeowners
refinanced half of all mortgages and the spread average fell to 1.5%, there would still be a 15% return. While
that could lead to a dividend reduction, the payout would still be large.
Refinancing concerns are even smaller for Capstead, since it focuses on adjustable-rate mortgages. The
homeowners behind such mortgages should feel less pressure to refinance because their payments would
also shrink if rates decline.
And prepayment could actually benefit REITs. So long as their stocks trade at a discount to book value, they
could use extra cash to buy back shares, boosting income for remaining shareholders.
Of course, REITs will eventually face tougher days when the Fed tightens. Higher short-term rates would
reduce their profitability and could hit their share prices hard, as happened in 2005.
But with an outright rate increase unlikely for a couple of years, the recent REIT massacre in panicked
markets has presented an opportunity.

Global Finance: Hedge Funds Get Positive Returns From Bearish Bets
Global Finance: Hedge Funds Get Positive Returns From Bearish Bets
By Neil Shah and Gregory Zuckerman
688 words
6 October 2011
The Wall Street Journal
J
C3
English
Copyright 2011 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Some of the world's largest hedge funds are finally seeing their bearish bets pay off as markets struggle.
Brevan Howard Asset Management LLP, one of Europe's biggest hedge-fund firms with $34 billion in assets,
is up nearly 13% in its flagship fund this year as of Sept. 23, according to a person close to the fund. The
outsize gains -- a rarity in a difficult year for hedge funds and much better than the loss of nearly 10% for the
Standard & Poor's 500-stock index in the same period -- are partly because of the London-based firm's
cautious approach to riskier investments tied to global economic growth.
Caxton Associates LP's $6.8 billion Global Investments Limited Fund, run by Andrew Law -- who takes the
helm of the $9.1 billion firm next year -- is up 0.67% this year through Oct. 3, after being down 3.25% at the
end of May, said a person close to the firm. The Global Investments fund rose 2.73% in August, 2.69% in
September and is up 0.4% so far this month.
The Caxton fund's "main gains over the past two months or so have been from trades benefiting from falling
growth expectations," this person said. Among the winning trades: Negative bets on interest rates in Brazil
and developed-world markets, bearish positions on gold and base metals, and a wager that the dollar would
rise against emerging-market and developed-world currencies.
Other "macro" hedge funds that bet on global financial markets and economic trends, such as Citadel
Investment Group, Tudor Investment Corp. and SAC Capital Advisors LP, also are showing positive returns
for the year as investors worried about the global economy dump once-high-flying investments like stocks,
emerging-market debt and gold. That compares to losses of about 8% for the average hedge fund this year.
Investors who put their cash in U.S. stocks would have lost about 6%.
SAC Capital Advisors, a $14 billion hedge fund run by Steve Cohen, was up about 6.5% this year through
September, after the firm's high investor expenses. SAC lost about 1.5% in September, according to
investors. People close to SAC said the firm shifted to a neutral position on the market several months ago,
worried about a looming debt crisis in Europe. Mr. Cohen believes stock-market valuations have become
more compelling and is encouraged that earnings estimates for 2012 have been reduced in recent days,
suggesting the market already is pricing in weakening conditions, according to someone familiar with his
thinking. But he isn't doing much buying, concerned that the situation in Europe could drag global markets still
lower.
And the largest fund managed by Kenneth Griffin's Citadel Investment Group managed to eke out a slight
gain in September, leaving it up about 15% this year.
Brevan Howard's flagship $24.3 billion fund, run by Alan Howard, was up 12.98% as of Sept. 23, compared
with a gain of 2.1% in early May, said a person familiar with the fund. Over the same period, the Dow Jones
Industrial Average has fallen about 14%, while the euro, considered a riskier currency, has tumbled 7%
against the dollar. The Brazilian real, closely tied to global economic growth and especially China's, has
depreciated nearly 14%.
Brevan Howard positioned its portfolio in anticipation of a global slowdown, according to a person familiar
with the matter. As global markets started to prepare for that possibility over the summer, the firm's
investments began to pay off, this person said.
But some believe macro hedge funds, especially those that speculate on currencies, have seen a pickup in
performance lately.
"Foreign-exchange as an asset class has done well throughout September," says Luca Avellini, a partner at
JW Partners, a Milan-based research and advisory firm for currency hedge funds. He says the 25
currencies-focused managers that his firm invests in were up roughly 2% in September as a group.

Metals, Crude Oil Slip on Bernanke, Growth Concerns

Precious metals slumped, with platinum and palladium falling to their lowest levels in more than a year, after
Federal Reserve Chairman Ben Bernanke signaled that the central bank isn't about to ride to the rescue of

Mr. Bernanke's testimony to a congressional panel, in which he said the Fed is "prepared to take further
action," initially helped metals, oil and stocks pull back from losses.
But Mr. Bernanke later said "we have no immediate plans to do anything like" a third round of bond buying,
known as quantitative easing, meaning the Fed won't be pumping more money into the system and devalue
the dollar.
Precious metals, especially gold, have gained for much of this year due to the weaker dollar after previous
efforts to stimulate the U.S. economy.
Oil and the metals also were under pressure as investors dumped commodities on the view that global
growth was slipping.
Worries that Europe's debt crisis would rattle global markets also weighed on prices.
The front-month gold contract, for October delivery, fell $41.30, or 2.5%, to settle at $1,614.70 a troy ounce
on the Comex division of the New York Mercantile Exchange.
The most actively traded platinum contract, for January delivery, settled down 3.2% to $1,468.60 a troy
ounce, a 21-month low. Palladium for December delivery fell 5%, to $564.15 an ounce, a 12-month low.
Light, sweet crude for November delivery settled down $1.94, or 2.5%, at $75.67 a barrel on the Nymex, the
lowest level since Sept. 23, 2010.
Other commodities markets rebounded from last week's losses as traders took advantage of the lower prices
to stock up.
Arabica coffee futures for December delivery settled 1.4% higher, at $2.2655 a pound on the
IntercontinentalExchange, while cocoa for December recovered from a more than two-year low to end at
$2,592 a ton, up 0.7% for the day. Cotton futures for December gained 2.6%, to settle at $1.0177 a pound.
But the gains might be brief, particularly for cotton, whose demand is tied to the health of the global economy.
"You're going to have plenty of selling on any bounces," said independent cotton analyst Mike Stevens.
"There's nothing to say that we've got a bottom on this thing."
New York cotton futures have shed 55% since hitting a record of $2.27 a pound in March.

Investing in Funds: A Quarterly Analysis --- A New 'Alternative' --- As managed-futures funds become available to small investors, here's...
Investing in Funds: A Quarterly Analysis --- A New 'Alternative' --- As managed-futures funds become
available to small investors, here's what buyers should know
By Marshall Eckblad
1,053 words
5 October 2011
The Wall Street Journal
J
C17
English
Copyright 2011 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Managed futures made a name for themselves in 2008, notching a solid gain as the stock market took a
sickening tumble. But at that time they were out of the reach of most investors, because they were available
almost exclusively through partnerships with relatively high minimum investments and net-worth
requirements.
Now, a rapidly growing number of mutual funds make managed futures easily obtainable, and small investors
are flocking to them.
Managed-futures funds invest in futures, swaps, options and other contracts whose values are tied to the
movement of various markets or market indicators, including stock indexes, interest rates, commodities,
precious metals and currencies. A given contract's value might depend on the price of gold relative to that of
copper, for example, or the likelihood that spreads between short-term and long-term interest rates will
narrow.
What buyers of managed-futures funds can expect is an investment that provides diversification for a portfolio
because it doesn't move in lockstep with stocks. What they shouldn't expect is that a managed-futures fund
will necessarily cushion any losses from stock investments as well as those partnerships did three years ago.
Or that these funds are immune to their own scary moments: While their low correlation with stock prices may
help balance a portfolio, managed-futures funds can be volatile, because the derivatives they invest in can be
volatile.
Managed-futures funds shouldn't be given too big a role in a portfolio, some analysts suggest. "They'll make
less money than your average long-only equity fund" during market rallies like those of 2009 and 2010, says
Todd Rosenbluth, a mutual-fund analyst at S&P Capital IQ, a unit of McGraw-Hill Cos. In stagnant markets,
he adds, their management fees -- which typically are about twice those of traditional stock funds -- will weigh
on performance. "If the market is flat, you may not get the same value as if the market is swinging more."
Investors should also be aware that managed-futures funds are under regulatory review, with the Commodity
Futures Trading Commission negotiating with the Securities and Exchange Commission as to how they'll
regulate the funds together. While some rule changes that might result would seem to benefit investors --
greater disclosure of fees and trading activity, for instance -- it's hard to predict the overall impact of the
review on the funds and their investors, because there is no certainty about what changes will emerge.
Managed-futures mutual funds currently hold about $7 billion, still just a speck on the mutual-fund industry's
$10 trillion landscape. But the sector's assets have tripled in the past year, and new funds are emerging at a
rapid clip.
Early entrant Rydex/SGI Managed Futures Strategy was launched in 2007. There are now 21 funds, with at
least a dozen more awaiting regulators' approval.
A large part of the funds' appeal is the history of managed futures moving independently of the stock market
-- the goal of portfolio diversification. Never was that independence more evident than in 2008: As the
Standard & Poor's 500-stock index fell 37% (including dividends), managed futures invested in private
accounts posted a 10% gain. The Rydex fund gained 8.5%.
The performance of managed-futures funds was far less spectacular in 2009 and 2010, as stocks surged. In
2010, managed-futures mutual funds tracked by Morningstar Inc. returned an average 3.8% while the S&P
gained 15.1%. But this year, as the S&P 500 fell 8.7% through September, the funds slid only 4.7%; in the
last three months, they fell just 1.2% as the S&P 500 dropped 13.9%.

--- Page 134 ---
Page 134 of 202 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Of course, as with any asset class, performance can vary greatly from one fund to another. But, unlike with
other mutual funds, it can be hard for investors to understand what's behind those differences. That's
because managed-futures funds aren't required to fully divulge their investments.
That also means it can be nearly impossible for investors to decipher exactly how much risk a portfolio
manager is taking with their money at any given moment. Although the SEC imposes broad limits on how
much risk the funds can take, investors have almost no way to know when and how a fund's risk level
fluctuates.
Managed-futures funds also aren't required to spell out all their fees. Typically, they publish management fees
totaling 2% to 3% annually of an investor's assets in the fund. But there can be substantial additional fees
that an investor will never see.
That's because the funds typically do much or all of their investing through offshore subsidiaries. That allows
them to obtain the most favorable tax treatment for investment gains. But some of those subsidiaries also pay
performance-based fees to investment advisers. And as long as those fees are paid within offshore accounts,
the funds aren't required to disclose them to investors.
To some investors that may seem like a moot point. Returns, after all, are calculated after total fees are
subtracted from investment results, so a 10% return, say, is a 10% return, whether a fund paid out substantial
performance fees to advisers or none at all. As long as the return is satisfactory, why worry about the details
of fees?
The main reason is risk. Performance-based fees can act as an incentive for an investment manager to take
bigger risks in search of bigger payoffs. Without knowing how much, if anything, a managed-futures fund is
paying out in such fees, it's impossible for investors to judge the risk involved.
Some funds have chosen to disclose more information than they are required to by current regulations. For
instance, AQR Capital Management, which manages the AQR Managed Futures Strategy fund, fully
discloses all fees and holdings. But others don't, and it isn't clear at this point if -- or when -- that will change.
The CFTC has proposed new rules for managed-futures funds.
---
Mr. Eckblad is a reporter in Chicago for Dow Jones Newswires and The Wall Street Journal. Email:
marshall.eckblad@dowjones.com.

Page 135 of 202 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Current Account: A Golden Age For Gold Loses Some of Its Luster
Current Account: A Golden Age For Gold Loses Some of Its Luster
By Francesco Guerrera
875 words
4 October 2011
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2011 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
The yellow metal is in a gray area.
For investors in commodities, the fourth quarter of 2011 opens on a cliffhanger: Will gold regain its cachet for
safety and resume its climb or has its glittering run come to an end?
It is a straightforward question after a month in which this famed "safe haven" underperformed a falling stock
market by some 50%.
The answer, though, is anything but straightforward and has to be found in gold's complex and tortuous
relationship with other investments.
Look at it this way: If the gyrations of commodities markets were to be turned into a movie, gold would be the
sad sack with unexpected powers of redemption, played by Paul Giamatti.
Inert and largely useless, in boom periods gold sits around in its drab living room moping, ignored by
investors and outshone by racier assets such as stocks and mortgage-backed securities.
But as economic gloom spreads and recession fears grip the world, gold, helped by its more-volatile buddy
silver (Owen Wilson), stages a triumphal comeback, riding to the rescue of scared fund managers.
I have a title for this tale of an unlikely hero for our troubled times: "No Yield" -- the feature that sets gold
apart from most other assets.
For the past decade, the script has been playing out pretty much as expected, with gold rising nearly sixfold
to a record $1,888.70 per troy ounce in August (not adjusting for inflation).
Then, a major plot twist. September was horrible, with gold prices falling 11% -- the most since the dark days
of October 2008. Much of the damage was done a fortnight ago, when gold recorded its worst week since
1983.
More worrying, the plunge occurred as stocks and other "risky" assets were falling, making the yellow metal a
decidedly unsafe haven.
Skeptics now are questioning gold's credentials as one of the few shelters from financial storms and some
investors have begun to wonder whether this is the beginning of the end for gold's spectacular surge.
In truth, nobody knows. Gold's very nature defies calculations of its "true" value. It has few economic uses
other than jewelry and some electronics, so its price is less influenced by supply and demand than other
metals.
And because gold yields no income, unlike bonds or stocks its worth can't be reduced to an Excel-friendly
equation.
The metal's penchant for evading precise valuations is, in many ways, its strength.
No expert can definitively tell whether gold is over- or undervalued, in a bubble or in a slump. So, over the
years, investors and central banks have bought gold for wildly different reasons: as a hedge against inflation,
an alternative to the dollar as a store of value, a blanket coverage against all of the world's ills or a pure
speculative play.
Call it reverse alchemy: Instead of looking for a "philosopher's stone" to transform other metals into gold like
their Middle Ages ancestors, investors have found a way to turn gold into whatever they want it to be.

--- Page 136 ---
Page 136 of 202 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
In practice, gold performed abysmally at some of its assignments. Take its best-known role, as a buffer
against inflation. Between 1980 and 2005, consumer prices in the U.S. more than doubled yet gold lost
some 20% of its value.
The link with stocks hasn't been perfect either. While gold tends to rise when stocks fall, the recent setback is
a reminder that there are exceptions to that rule.
A reason for gold's seemingly counterintuitive movements is that, when it rallies, investors faced with losses
on other assets raise funds by selling it.
There is some evidence that some of the sellers in the latest meltdown were indeed "stale bulls" -- the
unflattering market jargon for this strategy. Long-term investors such as exchange-traded funds, by contrast,
barely sold.
Research by James Steel at HSBC raises another possibility. Mr. Steel has unearthed a strong correlation
between gold's ups and downs and stocks' volatility. When shares go on a roller-coaster ride, so does gold.
Seen through this prism, gold looks at lot less "safe," at least in the short term. If the yellow metal is buffeted
just as stocks are, then investors trying to time the market are in for a rocky run.
This also would explain the stale-bull phenomenon. When the waves of a crisis mount, gold is the ballast that
gets thrown frantically into the sea to keep the ship afloat.
Add the fact that gold prices are sensitive to both current and expected interest rates -- the higher those
levels, the higher the cost of holding an asset that yields nothing -- and a clearer picture begins to emerge:
Gold's safety is in the eye of the beholder. The yellow metal naturally appeals to long-term investors with a
bleak view of the world while hot-money types might find its allure ephemeral.
Happy hunting for gold, but remember, no commodity market is based in Hollywood. Happy endings aren't
guaranteed.
---
Francesco Guerrera is The Wall Street Journal's Money & Investing editor. Write to him at:
currentaccount@wsj.com.

Only Agreement We Have on Taxation Is That It's a Mess
Letters to the Editor
Only Agreement We Have on Taxation Is That It's a Mess
582 words
4 October 2011
The Wall Street Journal
J
A20
English
Copyright 2011 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
John Steele Gordon's claim that the rich "largely escape" consumption taxes that are "simply added to the
price of goods sold" would be true only if high-income earners had a secret way of consuming less goods (or
"necessities") than do those with low earnings ("A Short History of the Income Tax," op-ed, Sept. 27). While
high-income earners have more disposable income after cost-of-living purchases, that would be true if there
were no consumption tax or if the consumption tax rate was tenfold higher. Consumption taxes are generally
more visible to citizens, making such a method of taxation less attractive to politicians who use such
pejoratives as "inherently regressive," as Mr. Gordon does, in describing a flat tax that is the same for
everyone.
As for income taxes, politicians somehow claim that taxing a small minority of its citizens at a much higher
rate than the vast majority is more "fair," and it's certainly more politically appealing. They use the term
progressive to try to put such unequal taxation in a positive light.
Daniel Zeidner
New Hartford, N.Y.
---
Unfortunately, both liberals and conservatives consistently ignore inflation and manipulation of interest
rates as an indirect tax. Historically this is a recent phenomenon. The Thomas Jefferson-Alexander Hamilton
disputes over the First Bank of the U.S. and William Jennings Bryan's "Cross of Gold" speech show
awareness of the political implication of currency manipulation.
Mr. Gordon indicates that in 1894 less than 1% of American households earned as much as $4,000 annually.
Since 1914, when the Federal Reserve was established to preserve the value of the dollar, the dollar has lost
more than 96 cents of its value. The saddest part is that this subterfuge taxation is inequitable and has
nothing to do with populism. Every senior citizen, rich or poor, has had over 80% of his dollar savings from
1960 confiscated. Until recently, sound financial planning assumed a rate of return of at least 4%. Elderly
retirees are forced to accept less than 1%, amounting to a 75% rate of taxation.
We have turned the old fable on its head. The grasshopper who borrows and overextends now can laugh at
the ant who seeks to save and provide for the winter.
Abraham Irwin
Passaic, N.J.
---
One of the first tax returns I prepared in the early 1970s was four pages short. My boss congratulated me on
my first real "complex" tax return. There was no state income tax.
Today, my average federal and state tax returns are 70 pages long-- and this does not include the 30 or more
worksheets that aren't filed but used for computations or as backup. This is very difficult to do with a
computer, almost impossible without one.
The immensely confusing alternative minimum tax, the totally absurd passive-loss computations and anything
having to do with educational deductions and credits are a disgrace to human intelligence.
This is not fixable. Fair and simple are inverse concepts. To be fair, you need carve-outs, special detailed
criteria, defined parameters and thresholds. To be simple, you need to make it, well, simple: no incentives, no
income thresholds, no exceptions, etc. Some will make out better than others.

--- Page 138 ---
Page 138 of 202 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
We need a totally new system, and of course, a path of transition to that system.
John A. Pfister, E.A.
Tax Accountant
St. Augustine, Fla.
